On Thursday 31 August 2006 22:56, Russell Senior wrote:
> You don't have to rebuild anything, you just boot your Ubuntu Breezy
> Live-CD on a box of your choice, figure out which package you want to
> add to your pyramid image, run:
> 
> ? dpkg -L <package-name>
> 
> and look at the file list. ?E.g.:
> 
> ? $ dpkg -L bridge-utils
> ? /.
> ? /etc
<snip>
> ? /usr/share/man/man8
> ? /usr/share/man/man8/brctl.8.gz
> 
> Figure out which ones are needed (basically /usr/sbin/brctl, and
> /etc/network/if*/bridge), and copy them over to your pyramid image
> (mount it somewhere and copy away, using utility of your choice).
> Test and... Viola! ?Package (or the interesting bits anyway)
> installed!
> 
> You need to test because brctl might depend on something not already
> present (I vaguely remember a library, ldd helps here). ?Rinse and
> repeat. ?You might also need a kernel module, not sure. ?A few
> iterations and you are done. ?Not. ?That. ?Hard.

I was paying attention when you said that before. :) I wanted the new release 
with all the shiny new stuff- new kernel, kernel modules, associated baggage 
like /boot and /etc and /lib and such, and not just a few files. So I 
upgraded the existing installation, and I think it's pretty darn slick to be 
able to do that.

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday Sep 7, 2006
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************
          
                                 PRESENTATION

                                   Inkscape

                                      by

                               Bryce Harrington
                               <bryce at osdl.org>
 
      Inkscape is an Open Source vector graphics editor, with capabilities 
      similar to Illustrator, Freehand, CorelDraw, or Xara X using the  
      W3C standard Scalable Vector Graphics  (SVG) file format. 
      Supported SVG features include shapes, paths, text, markers, 
      clones, alpha blending, transforms, gradients, patterns, and  
      grouping. Inkscape also supports Creative Commons meta-data, 
      node editing, layers, complex path operations, bitmap tracing, 
      text-on-path, flowed text, direct XML editing, and more. It imports 
      formats such as JPEG, PNG, TIFF, and others and exports PNG as 
      well as multiple vector-based formats.

      Inkscape's main goal is to create a powerful and convenient drawing 
      tool fully compliant with XML, SVG, and CSS standards. We also aim 
      to maintain a thriving user and developer community by using open, 
      community-oriented development process, and by making sure Inkcape 
      is easy to learn, to use, and to extend.

Ideas?


From plug at merlot.com  Fri Sep  1 21:11:45 2006
From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)
Date: Fri, 1 Sep 2006 21:11:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Need source on cheap UPS solutions
In-Reply-To: <55cf303a0609011429t11a3af7ja9061822b87f8628@mail.gmail.com>
References: <55cf303a0609011429t11a3af7ja9061822b87f8628@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20060902041145.GL16172@merlot.com>

Alan Olsen (alan.olsen at gmail.com) typed this ...
> I need to obtain a small cheap UPS that will handle a couple of small
> routers for a couple of minutes at a time.
> 
> I am looking for something reliable.  My last UPS lated about a year and a
> half and then just refused to hold a charge.  (And the batteries cost about
> as much as the unit.)

You want cheap AND reliable? Well, what's 'cheap' to you? I'd suggest
that you get a big enough UPS that it does power conditioning. I think
APC does that from 650 up, on the professional (i.e. non-home) models.

I have an old rackmount APC UPS, not very big power-wise but it can run
my network and a lightweight server for about 20 minutes safely. I
bought it refurbished, and have replaced the batteries once in 7 years.

So even though I paid more for it, I'll bet that replacing batteries
every 18 months would make your cost about the same as mine. 

Of course, my wife's PC is plugged into a cheapo UPS, and it has been
far more reliable than I expected it to be, 5 years and counting. 

Pay for quality, or hope to be lucky, I guess.

--Kurt

From brmiller at kudzuforest.com  Fri Sep  1 21:17:04 2006
From: brmiller at kudzuforest.com (Brendan Miller)
Date: Fri, 1 Sep 2006 21:17:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Pebble, Pyramid, Puppy, patheticness on my picomachine
Message-ID: <20060902041704.GA13710@couv>


With the recent discussions on Pebble and Pyramid Linux, I wondered how
suitable these were for "generic" x86 hardware (as opposed to an
outright embedded environment).  I have a Neoware thin client with 64MB
IDE flash DOM, it's integrated Rtl8139 (or whatever) 10/100 NIC, and a
PCI slot with a Ralink RT2500-based 802.11g card.  I would like to make
this box into a wireless router/gateway device between my wired lan and
a wireless network.

I have done work on the Neoware boxen in the past, and their
RedHat-derivative NeoLinux is pretty decent, albeit requiring a specific
build environment.  I have an rt2500 driver built for this box that
associates and works, but I have no iptables so it's kind of hard to do
NAT/forwarding/et.al. without it.

I could set up a build environment for the box and hack away, but I was
kind of hoping to get this project running sooner than later.  Do either
Pebble or Pyramid support the rt2500?  I've looked at Puppy too, but a
lot of these small linux distros want to boot from a CD.  The Neobox has
USB, so I could boot from a USB floppy (which I have), but I dont' have
a USB CDROM.  It can PXE boot too--so I could set up some shenanigan
using that.

Basically, it comes down to the fact that I am lazy, er--I have two kids
under two years old, and no time to work on this, and just need it to
work.  Anyone have any Quick Routes to Routing Bliss that should work on
this hardware?

TIA
Brendan

If you have an existing Linux system then you could use the buildroot
project.  It will build
up a small system that has the packages you configure and does most of
the startup
and configuration of services.  It does have a limitation in that your
Linux box needs
an internet connection as it downloads the needed packages after you
run the config.
If you can work through a kernel build then you can work through the
buildroot.  You
will have to download and build your own kernel also.

The soekris hardware use "generic x86" instructions.  Some of the
hardware Pebble/Pyramid is intended to run on is 486-like and some is
pentium-like.  It ought to work on generic x86 hardware.  I got
Pyramid booting on a Fujitsu Stylistic.

If it helps any, I have written up some of the procedure I used in
hacking on one of our pebble-derived images:

  <http://wiki.personaltelco.net/index.cgi/MississippiMetrixConfiguration>

Not all of the things I refer to are immediately available to the
general public (mostly things locally hacked), but it gives you some
ideas how it can work.  It is mostly my notes on how I got things
working so I can repeat it all after I've forgotten in a week.

If your wireless hardware works in Ubuntu, then you can follow my
general instructions for Pyramid... copying the files needed from an
Ubuntu Live-CD environment.

From russell at personaltelco.net  Sat Sep  2 00:11:03 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 02 Sep 2006 00:11:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] fluke IR189USB support?
In-Reply-To: <86iro1aibt.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86iro1aibt.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <86sljan3p4.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Russell" == Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> writes:

Russell> I am looking at capturing data from one of these nice Fluke
Russell> 189 Digital Multimeters.  This particular one has an infrared
Russell> interface and an optional cable to connect to a USB port so
Russell> that you can interrogate the device for its current reading
Russell> (our data collection protocol currently has us entering an
Russell> unstable value by hand, which is proving to be quite
Russell> tedious).

Russell> Does anyone have experience with this cable, and do you know
Russell> if it works with recent linux kernels.  It is probably some
Russell> kind of usb-serial device inside, but I haven't had my hands
Russell> on one yet (got one on order).  I've found the protocol for
Russell> querying the device, so it really just boils down to the
Russell> details of being able to talk across the link.

Just a follow up on this.  Today I finally got the IR189USB cable and
a Fluke 189 together in the same building.  And then, in the last
hour, I got them together in the same room, and lo and behold: it
works!

I had discovered a month or two ago that the IR189USB was likely to
work.  I plugged it into a USB port and it got detected as a pl2303,
which is a well-supported USB/serial converter chip.  I found some
documentation from Fluke on the communication protocol here:

  <http://cfa-www.harvard.edu/~thunter/manuals/RemoteSpec89_18X.htm>

and when I type QM into a suitably configured minicom terminal, I get
a reading back out.  Pretty coolsville.

As usual, I discovered some under-reported things in the process.  The
IR link is not some TV-remote like thing, that can beam data across
the room as I had expected.  I wondered how multiple DMMs would be
discriminated in such an environment.  Instead, the Fluke 189 has a
little jig that holds the IR part of the cable directly against the
top of the meter, meaning one-cable/one-fluke189.

But, once I got gpsd to let go of /dev/ttyUSB0 (thank you mr.
/usr/bin/lsof), it works just fine.  Hope this helps someone,
somewhere, sometime.  Yay for the Hands-On Imperative!


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

> top of the meter, meaning one-cable/one-fluke189.

Okay, maybe I'm missing something, but exactly what advantage does
using IR give you when the IR has to be attached to the meter?

Jason

>>>>> "Jason" == Jason Martin <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> writes:

Russell> As usual, I discovered some under-reported things in the
Russell> process.  The IR link is not some TV-remote like thing, that

Russell> how multiple DMMs would be discriminated in such an
Russell> environment.  Instead, the Fluke 189 has a little jig that

Russell> the meter, meaning one-cable/one-fluke189.

Jason> does using IR give you when the IR has to be attached to the
Jason> meter?

Electrical isolation for one thing.  No extraneous signals messing up
your measurement.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Sun Sep  3 22:26:34 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Sun, 3 Sep 2006 22:26:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] pyramid vs. Berling ucLibc
Message-ID: <200609032226.34836.carla@bratgrrl.com>

Pinging Russell, or any interested personages.... I meant to follow up on 
the "ick" directed at Bering ucLibc, and was wondering why? I like that 
Pyramid is not modified very much from Ubuntu, which makes it easy for 
non-coding geniuses to hack. On the other hand, Bering uses all kinds of 
modified libs so you get the small size from reduce lib sizes, and it has 
package management. And it has a much prettier web site. :)

From russell at personaltelco.net  Mon Sep  4 01:16:07 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 04 Sep 2006 01:16:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] pyramid vs. Berling ucLibc
In-Reply-To: <200609032226.34836.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200609032226.34836.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <86veo4dp2w.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Carla" == Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> writes:

Carla> Pinging Russell, or any interested personages.... I meant to
Carla> follow up on the "ick" directed at Bering ucLibc, and was
Carla> wondering why? 

Only because, compared to pebble/pyramid, my experience with Bering is
limited to a brush associated with the stock firmware on the Netgear
WGT634U.  It isn't an "ick" you should rely on, just a personal
involuntary convulsion.  Sorry about that.

The stock wgt firmware using a LEAF Bering derived system.  It is
interesting in that the system unpacks itself from tarballs into a
ramdisk as it boots up.  Which is interesting and cool in its own way,
but it does result in a system that takes 2 minutes to boot on the
wgt.  Not sure how much that taints the system you were referring to.

One alternative on the wgt is OpenWgt, which boots in about 15
seconds.  It (iirc) using a jffs2 filesystem.  OpenWrt using a couple
different strategies, one is a combination SquashFS/Jffs2 which boots
in about 30 seconds (the jffs2 file system here is the rootfs and
contains symbolic links into the read-only squashfs tree).


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

came up reporting that fsck needed to be run on the partition.

it was rebooted the partion came up as read-only so the mount point ( 
/var/log ) is none writeable.

I can take the partition out of the loop but I'm hoping it might just be 
my "remote eyes" missed something. :-)

Suggestions, clues?


TIA,
Rod

From nixlists at writemoore.net  Mon Sep  4 10:28:33 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)
Date: Mon, 04 Sep 2006 10:28:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Personalized Search Page Generator?
In-Reply-To: <20060820105754.GA1530@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20060820105754.GA1530@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <44FC6241.1050709@writemoore.net>

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> The top 10 or so of my browser bookmarks are for search engines:  
> Google, Wikipedia, Addall(books), Switchboard, etc.  It would not
> seem to difficult to build a local html (or if necessary, Java)
> webpage that I can use as a homepage and search all these different
> services by clicking different activation buttons.  Not a customized
> portal page elsewhere, a page that is stored on my own machine that
> I control.
>
> I am not looking for the usual RSS feed pages, lots of content but
> few data entry boxes.
>
> If such a thing is possible, someone has probably already thought of
> it, and a tool to design such customized home pages may be out there
> on the web.  Any suggestions?
>   


Have you seen Rollyo?  It's not exactly what you're talking about, but 
it does allow you to create customized searches using only the search 
engines you specify.  So, for example, if you have three or four book 
sites you like to search, you could make a Rollyo that searches all of 
them with one query.  I've seen it embedded in websites and blogs.  You 
can also add it to Firefox.

http://rollyo.com/about.html

-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Mon Sep  4 16:31:59 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Mon, 4 Sep 2006 17:31:59 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Library Extension, '.lai'
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041533030.24836@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041533030.24836@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041706260.19959@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

Initial googling seems to indicate that libtool creates .lai files during
installs.  Some more links, similar to the one you found:

http://lists.kde.org/?l=kde-devel&m=95811214125332&q=raw
http://www.mail-archive.com/libtool at gnu.org/msg08899.html
http://www.handhelds.org/hypermail/oe/50/5004.html
http://oss.sgi.com/archives/xfs/2002-03/msg00438.html

It seems that libtool creates .lai files.  Not sure why.  I can only
find one such file on my gentoo system, and it is in the portage tmp
directory.  I would guess that libtool creates these for in-process use,
but they do not get installed into the libs directory.  Mine is part of
the gnome-cups-manager ebuild.

Here's a link that, while not explaining anything, indicates that .lai
files are really .la files.  It shows 3 examples of an .lai file being
directly copied to an .la file:

http://sourceware.org/autobook/autobook/autobook_80.html

Carlos Konstanski

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041808220.19959@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

Looks like you're in the same sorry boat as all those other people who
lament similarly in their forum posts.  One time, on my gentoo box, I
had to re-emerge libtool for some reason that I have since forgotten (I
don't think it had anything to do with .lai files).  Maybe you might
give this a shot?  libtool is an abstraction layer for hiding the
grotesqueries of a system's linking implementation behind a common API.
Perhaps the implementation has changed on your debian box since the last
libtool upgrade.  You'd think they would have updated libtool if this
were the case, but there are humans in charge of these things.  Perhaps
the testing version of libtool?

Carlos Konstanski

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Mon Sep  4 19:06:08 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Mon, 4 Sep 2006 20:06:08 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: emacs/SLIME afficianados?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041816340.19959@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041816340.19959@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609041943010.19959@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I demonstrated poor troubleshooting skills here.  I should clarify.  The
issue isn't emacs or slime, it's SBCL and my lack of knowledge o how to
use it.  I get the same problems in an SBCL image launched from a shell.
The subject of this thread should be "SBCL afficianados?" or "CMUCL
afficianados or derivatives thereof, especially SBCL?"

But who develops lisp code in a shell-launched SBCL when emacs/SLIME is
so much nicer?  So the only place I ever see my issues is in this
environment.  You can see where I made my error in judgement.

When I say "system packages", I mean libraries like cl-sql, cl-ppcre,
cl-uffi, etc.  These are all asdf packages, and they all compile into
/var/cache/common-lisp-controller/.  Gentoo (my distro) uses the same
common-lisp-controller as debian.  Maybe this is the defining difference
between code that behaves correctly in the debugger vs. my code, or maybe
not.  It's one factor to consider.  The other factor is that I do not
compile .fasl files in my project code; I simply load the files into the
running lisp.  Intuition tells me that this should make it easier for the
debugger to find the source for the code in a backtrace because it does
not have to compare source to .fasl, but I may be wrong.

In my project files, I have a "top-level" source file that I load, and
it contains (load "<filename>.lisp") forms that handle the loading of the
next group of files, and they load more files in turn, until all the
files are loaded.  I will experiment with (compile "<filename>.lisp")
followed by (load "<filename>.fasl"), and see if that makes any
difference.

I'm writing website code, which is why I don't want to treat it like an
asdf package, putting it in /usr/share/common-lisp/source/.  I don't
want to point webserver document-roots at root-owned paths.
Using common-lisp-controller is more acceptable because the cache is
owned by the owner of the lisp image, and its location is hidden from
the webserver behind an interface.

Carlos Konstanski

> seen the actual messages ) and when the system was rebooted it came up 
> reporting that fsck needed to be run on the partition.
>

> be fine but a few hours later it went bonkers again and when it was rebooted

If you are having write errors then you may have a drive failing.

Look at the end of /var/log/messages on that machine and see what kind of 
errors you are getting.

Expect to replace the drive real soon now.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sat Sep  2 17:21:22 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Sat,  2 Sep 2006 17:21:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iptables firewalling...
Message-ID: <1157242882.44fa200231796@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Is there any standard service that an Internet client should be able to
access between tcp/udp ports 1024 and 65535 on my server?  I'm thinking 
no, so I have this rule in my firewall:

iptables -A PREROUTING -t mangle -p tcp -i $global_interface \
         -d $out_address --dport 1024:65535 --syn -j DROP

I am not running a proxy server, web server, or ftp server on
this Internet connected machine.  This machine does run X, so I
don't want that to become a security problem.  NFS and RPC in
general bind to non root ports, but those aren't supposed to
be available from my server to anyone on the Net.  My rule
doesn't cover udp, but udp apparently doesn't have a 
connection flag so I don't know what to do there.

Another curiosity, why might someone or a bot for that matter 
want to connect to a very low numbered tcp/udp port such as 2 
or even port 0?

Michael Robinson

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Sep  5 10:14:26 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 5 Sep 2006 11:14:26 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] CRT dark...
In-Reply-To: <1157069485.44f77aade3556@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1157069485.44f77aade3556@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051104290.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I've never seen a monitor that was NOT compatible with linux.  You just
want to look up the specs for the monitor and see if it'll do whatever
resolution X refresh rate you want.  Then you need to get the horizontal
and vertical refresh rates for the monitor, and put those figures into
your xorg.conf file.  Has anybody ever had this NOT work for a
particular monitor?

I've certainly seen monitors that can display 1600x1200, but only at
60hz, which is unacceptably flickery.  The specs for the monitor will
plainly indicate this.

I'm no expert on these things, but isn't the interface between the video
card and monitor completely divorced from the interface between the
kernel and video card?  In other words, shouldn't this be a question of
compatibility between a monitor and a video card, with no mention at all
of the OS?

Carlos Konstanski

On 9/2/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> Is there any standard service that an Internet client should be able to
> access between tcp/udp ports 1024 and 65535 on my server?  I'm thinking
> no, so I have this rule in my firewall:
That depends on what you consider a standard internet client.
Take a look at your /etc/services

>
> iptables -A PREROUTING -t mangle -p tcp -i $global_interface \
>          -d $out_address --dport 1024:65535 --syn -j DROP
>

> I am not running a proxy server, web server, or ftp server on
> this Internet connected machine.  This machine does run X, so I
> don't want that to become a security problem.  NFS and RPC in
> general bind to non root ports, but those aren't supposed to
> be available from my server to anyone on the Net.  My rule
> doesn't cover udp, but udp apparently doesn't have a
> connection flag so I don't know what to do there.
NFS runs across udp so if you don't block it and you have shares
someone could possible soft mount your share and sponge your files.
>
> Another curiosity, why might someone or a bot for that matter
> want to connect to a very low numbered tcp/udp port such as 2
> or even port 0?
>
To check your security.  To determine if there is a machine there.

On 9/5/06, Carlos Konstanski <ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com> wrote:
>
> I've never seen a monitor that was NOT compatible with linux.  You just
> want to look up the specs for the monitor and see if it'll do whatever
> resolution X refresh rate you want.  Then you need to get the horizontal
> and vertical refresh rates for the monitor, and put those figures into
> your xorg.conf file.  Has anybody ever had this NOT work for a
> particular monitor?
>
> I've certainly seen monitors that can display 1600x1200, but only at
> 60hz, which is unacceptably flickery.  The specs for the monitor will
> plainly indicate this.
>
> I'm no expert on these things, but isn't the interface between the video
> card and monitor completely divorced from the interface between the
> kernel and video card?  In other words, shouldn't this be a question of
> compatibility between a monitor and a video card, with no mention at all
> of the OS?



It could also be an issue with the gamma settings.

What kind of video card?  If you are using the nVIDIA commercial drivers,
have you mucked with the settings?

Does the monitor have a brightness and contract setting that needs to be
adjusted?  Sometimes they get reset if there is a preset and children play
with the buttons.


From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Sep  5 10:22:52 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 5 Sep 2006 11:22:52 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] iptables firewalling...
In-Reply-To: <1157242882.44fa200231796@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1157242882.44fa200231796@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051115410.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

Lots.  XDMCP, vnc, cvspserver, etc.  Look in /etc/services for an
incomplete list.

Of course, you would drop packets for all ports first, and then accept
specific ports afterward.  Therefore, you would only be concerned with
those ports on which you had services running.

Then there's the distinction between LAN firewall (FORWARD chain) and
WAN firewall.  You would allow tcp port 3128 (squid) on your LAN, but
not to the WAN.  Same with database socket ports.

Carlos Konstanski

From dave.houston at gmail.com  Tue Sep  5 11:02:43 2006
From: dave.houston at gmail.com (Dave Houston)
Date: Tue, 05 Sep 2006 11:02:43 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] CRT dark...
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051104290.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <1157069485.44f77aade3556@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051104290.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <1157479363.7954.1.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Tue, 2006-09-05 at 11:14 -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> I'm no expert on these things, but isn't the interface between the video
> card and monitor completely divorced from the interface between the
> kernel and video card?  In other words, shouldn't this be a question of
> compatibility between a monitor and a video card, with no mention at all
> of the OS?


For the most part, yes.  There are some special instances where the
video card driver and X(.org) need to know about how the monitor is
plugged in -- usually in a dual-headed environment, or on a card with
both a DVI and a VGA output (and you're using both).  But otherwise,
yes.  

We wouldn't get very far into the boot process if monitors didn't work
without the OS telling them what to do. ;)

-- 
Dave Houston <dave.houston at gmail.com>


From jonscully at gmail.com  Tue Sep  5 11:07:32 2006
From: jonscully at gmail.com (Jon Scully)
Date: Tue, 5 Sep 2006 11:07:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iptables firewalling...
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051124200.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <1157242882.44fa200231796@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051124200.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <53107f6e0609051107if1f105p86f2d193e341b48e@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/5/06, Carlos Konstanski <ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com> wrote:
> Another thought on ports and security.  here's something I need to do,
> right this minute.  I have port 22 open, but I accept password
> authentication for SSH.  I need to take away password auth immediately,
> and require authorized keys instead.  You should see the syslog entries
> - thousands of attempts to guess usernames and passwords.  Of all the
> logs I see, this one worries me the most.
>
> Carlos Konstanski

Using keys will add extra security, but it won't stop the log files
from filling up.  Lately it seems a whole dictionary is being used for
these attacks (it used to be just a dozen words at a time: root,
admin, apache, john, etc.).  I guess using a whole dictionary has been
more successful for these guys.

At least it seemed to work for one client's server.  He created a user
account using a common name and used the same name as the password.
There were about 3GB of outgoing e-mail choked up in the spool, by the
time I got there (SMTP port was blocked).  Apparently when these
attempts are successful, the attacking script just pushes another
generic mailer script out and kicks it off blind.  (Was a simple Perl
script.)

But back to the log files...  The only way to stop these attacks from
filling up your log files is to block port 22.  You can do this and
open up the port for just a few specific IP addresses.  Or you can
close it for all IP addresses and dynamically open it up for specific
ones using port knocking.

Some would say port knocking isn't a worthy security measure.  Perhaps
so.  However, having clean log files is still a wonderful thing. ;-)

Here's a shameless plug for a port-knocking technique you can use
anywhere iptables is available: http://www.soloport.com/iptables.html


From carla at bratgrrl.com  Tue Sep  5 11:20:08 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Tue, 5 Sep 2006 11:20:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] iptables firewalling...
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051124200.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <1157242882.44fa200231796@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051124200.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <200609051120.08904.carla@bratgrrl.com>

On Tuesday 05 September 2006 10:26, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Another thought on ports and security. ?here's something I need to do,
> right this minute. ?I have port 22 open, but I accept password
> authentication for SSH. ?I need to take away password auth immediately,
> and require authorized keys instead. ?You should see the syslog entries
> - thousands of attempts to guess usernames and passwords. ?Of all the
> logs I see, this one worries me the most.
> 
> Carlos Konstanski

That will increase your security, but your poor ole logfiles are still going 
to get hammered. A good tool for protecting both your system and your 
logfiles is Denyhosts. http://denyhosts.sourceforge.net/

It's easy to use. Denyhosts blocks bogus ssh login attempts, and protects 
other services too, by parsing your logs and then writing rules 
to /etc/hosts.deny, all according to whatever criteria you set.

?Carla Schroder

?and sysadmin's guide! http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Sep  5 12:21:36 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 5 Sep 2006 13:21:36 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] iptables firewalling...
In-Reply-To: <200609051120.08904.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <1157242882.44fa200231796@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051124200.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
	<200609051120.08904.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051300400.2167@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

DenyHosts is in the gentoo portage tree.  I am sold.

I do like the soloport solution too, but I get a cheap thrill from
identifying and denying specific jerks.  Getting an email that says
something like, "I just denied this jerk for you" will certainly bring a
smile to my face.  I used to spend a half hour every weekend denying
jerks manually.

Both these solutions address ssh only.  At first, one might wish for a
more comprehensive tool.  But I have not seen many other attacks that are
anywhere near as worrisome.  Putting apache behind pound was a good move
because pound rejects fishy requests.  But those attacks were all aimed
at IIS anyway, what with "...xxxxxxxxxxxxcmd.exe" style attempts at
getting a shell, or "../../../../WINDOWS/etcetc" attempts to walk into
the WINDOWS directory.  IIS is so lame that it does not support the
concept of a document-root jail.

You'd think script kiddies would add a bit to their scripts that would
determine the OS of the victim before launching IIS attacks, port 1433
scans, etc.  I'd have more respect for them if they displayed more
intelligence (but only a little tiny bit more).  I guess the hackers we
should worry about are the ones who do not leave a paper trail a mile
long in syslog, like cross-site script attackers.  If I were a hacker, I
would not go after the most secure application known to man.  I would go
after hole-ridden webapps.  Maybe it's time to pay closer attention to
database logs, and start logging all queries, and validating the
bejeezus out of dynamic SQL, and escaping all form input before
redisplaying it in a web page or stashing it in a database table.

Carlos Konstanski

> so.  However, having clean log files is still a wonderful thing. ;-)
> 
> Here's a shameless plug for a port-knocking technique you can use
> anywhere iptables is available: http://www.soloport.com/iptables.html 

   I agree that portknocking alone is weak but all good security has
layers. When portknocking is used in combination with rsa authentication
and strong username and passwords it certainly helps. If portknocking
allows you to be invisible to 90% of the script kiddies out there then
all the better. Plus your logs will thank you...
   Below is a little variation of the same portknocking idea. Its a bit
more simplistic but I like it that way. If I don't have to build scripts
to knock from remote hosts then I am happy. Just telnet to the central
port and boom you're in. If a fella wanted to build scripts to connect
and make it difficult then he might as well do encrypted icmp
portknocking or something fancy like that. I am all about simplicity
myself and yes I have to connect from other systems like windows boxes.
   Funny thing is I originally got it from the same website. Can't find
the old link though... 
   Keep in mind that you will want to have the --remove statements above
and below the port number you use for access. This way if there are any
crawling access attempts they will end up closing the port no matter
which direction they scan.
   To use just telnet to the unlock port(6423) and break the telnet
connection. This will open 22 for your ip address. Then when you are
done just telnet to the lock port(either 6422 or 6424).

#SSH PORTKNOCKING:
iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 22 -m
recent --rcheck --name SSHAccess -j ACCEPT
iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 6422 -m
recent --name SSHAccess --remove -j DROP
iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 6423 -m
recent --name SSHAccess --set -j DROP
iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 6424 -m
recent --name SSHAccess --remove -j DROP

   



From jonscully at gmail.com  Tue Sep  5 13:45:23 2006

Subject: [PLUG] Re: iptables firewalling...

> iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 22 -m
> recent --rcheck --name SSHAccess -j ACCEPT
> iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 6422 -m
> recent --name SSHAccess --remove -j DROP
> iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 6423 -m
> recent --name SSHAccess --set -j DROP
> iptables -A INPUT -m state --state NEW -m tcp -p tcp --dport 6424 -m
> recent --name SSHAccess --remove -j DROP
>

Well, the current method is really a state machine, so the
distribution of ports is irrelevant.  For example, if one knocks on
6424, then 6422 to get in, any other sequence brings it back to state
zero.  So, scanning 6424 then 6423 already reverts it to state zero.
It has to be exact or it collapses.

Pretty sure I wrote the original article (the one you can't find) and
later discarded it for the new, better (me thinks) technique.

You can still use telnet with the new rules. I just got tired of
typing so much and wrote the script you see in the article.  (Guess I
could do 'telnet <host> <port1>; sleep 2; kill $PID' etc. instead of
using 'nmap <timeout flags> ...'. Some day... as nmap isn't always
available.)

needed to make it work.  You pretty much disable effective password security
on the shares when you do this BTW.  (Win98 has some serious problems in
authentication for Samba shares that make it trivially crackable.)

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Sep  5 16:56:53 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 5 Sep 2006 17:56:53 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Samba/Windows 98/XP browse protocol
In-Reply-To: <44FD77AF.1070204@alcpress.com>
References: <44FD77AF.1070204@alcpress.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051742430.21764@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I don't recall seeing this behavior in the last mixed network I was in,
which utilized a linux box as the master browser.  We used settings like
these in smb.conf:

     os level = 99
     preferred master = Yes
     domain master = Yes
     local master = Yes
     enhanced browsing = Yes
     wins support = Yes

Although looking at the official samba docs, it seems that they use:

     os level = 65

instead of 99.

I'm not seeing any definition of "enhanced browsing" at the moment, but
I seem to recall it has something to do with older windows vs. newer
windows.  In the network where these settings were used, both 98 and XP
boxes could browse.

I used to spend many hours watching BROWSER packets in ethereal in our
network of 100+ computers.  Every time something went wrong with
browsing, it would be found in this manner, and it invariably was a
rogue machine with el-crappo WINS settings.  We dished out WINS settings
via DHCP, but if a windows box has hardcoded values, it will use those
instead.  One such windows box will take down the whole ship.  You ought
to look for machines that have bogus WINS server settings, or are set to
broadcast unnecessarily.  Look for machines trying to take over as
master browser.  There will be a tug-of-war between the real master
browser and the rogue box.

I have since moved on to a new company, which is a windows shop all the
way (how I got them to let me build my workstation with linux is still a
mystery).  Therefore, I have no access to a mixed samba network any more
(my home network is 100% linux, and no samba).  So I'm riding on pure
memory here.  Hope I didn't steer you wrong.

Carlos Konstanski

>From: Jeff Moore <Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu>
>    I agree that portknocking alone is weak but all good security has
> layers. When portknocking is used in combination with rsa authentication
> and strong username and passwords it certainly helps. If portknocking
> allows you to be invisible to 90% of the script kiddies out there then
> all the better. Plus your logs will thank you...

The ircony of course is that the whole point of logging is to catch
things like this. You *want* to know that host x.y.z.w is an evil guy.
The issue is that you don't want nearly so many log entries. This strikes
me as a case for connection limiting at the firewall (say 5 connections
every 3 minutes per host), and changing sshd so that it quickly starts
rejecting connections from a host that repeatedly fails.

Agreed.  Hack attempts should be logged.  No onw should be able to
attempt a breakin and get by unnoticed.  Who cares how big the logs
get; that's what logrotate is for.  It would be nice to be able to move
certain entries to a different log file, though.  The One Big Syslog
model is a little dated.  DenyHosts writes logs to its own file, and it
writes plenty of them, and it looks like a very easily parsable format.

I'm still waiting for a "return customer" to see how DenyHosts logs a
hack attempt from an IP that is already in hosts.deny.

Carlos Konstanski

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Tue Sep  5 19:29:37 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)

On Tuesday 05 September 2006 17:57, Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> The ircony of course is that the whole point of logging is to catch
> things like this. You *want* to know that host x.y.z.w is an evil guy.
> The issue is that you don't want nearly so many log entries. This strikes
> me as a case for connection limiting at the firewall (say 5 connections
> every 3 minutes per host), and changing sshd so that it quickly starts
> rejecting connections from a host that repeatedly fails.
> 

Connection limiting in iptables is annoying and just asking for trouble. You 
end up writing whitelist rules to let legitimate users in without delays, and 
it turns into a mess. DenyHosts handles it elegantly and simply.
http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/netos/article.php/3553111

Knockd is a nice port-knocking implementation that is also easy to use,
http://www.serverwatch.com/tutorials/article.php/3625276

Both work for any service.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Wed Sep  6 09:49:40 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Wed, 6 Sep 2006 09:49:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] partition mounts read-only
In-Reply-To: <44FC9171.3090001@acm.org>
References: <44FC48F3.9080503@acm.org> <44FC9171.3090001@acm.org>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0609060949s1a592738w7a5b90d1d8c71331@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/4/06, Roderick A. Anderson <raanders at acm.org> wrote:
> Ack!! Nothing but bad news.  Well I felt it might be this but was hoping
> otherwise.
>
> Thanks to all.  Fortunately it is only the /var/log partition.  I can
> grab the important files ( a mail spool wit hsome enhancements :-) and
> get a new HD installed.
>

Check the logs.  If there was an I/O error that caused the kernel to
remount the partition, then it should be recorded.  Since it is the
/var/log partition, you might not have anything.  It could have been a
kernel panic or transient I/O error instead of a hard drive error.

Try running SMART diagnostics.  Modern drives will record the errors
and lots of other diagnostics.  The smartctl program (in the
smartmontools package on RH) can query them.  When hard drives report
read and write errors to the OS, they are usually ready to fail
because they have already gone through all their bad sectors.

 - Ian


From kens at cad2cam.com  Wed Sep  6 21:40:51 2006
From: kens at cad2cam.com (Kenneth G. Stephens)
Date: Wed, 06 Sep 2006 21:40:51 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Evolution 2.6.3 Not showing all messages
In-Reply-To: <1157173920.3797.2.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1157173920.3797.2.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <1157604051.20957.14.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>

On Fri, 2006-09-01 at 22:12 -0700, Keith wrote:
> I have about 5000 messages in one email folder however I can only see a
> couple of hundred of them. I have tried the show hidden messages
> settings and other settings but the messages do not display. 
> 
> TNX for any help or tips. 
> 

Check in your ~/.evolution/mail/local directory and delete the *.cmeta,
*.ev-summary, *.ibex.*  file.  This will force evolution to rebuild the
indexes.

If you are still having problems with mail, then go into the
~/.evolution/mail/spool/var/spool/$USER dirctory and remove the files
there.  That is if $USER is your login.

HTH,

Ken 
CAD 2 CAM





From dpool at hevanet.com  Thu Sep  7 07:58:19 2006
From: dpool at hevanet.com (David Pool)
Date: Thu, 07 Sep 2006 07:58:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Sed and POSIX character class question
Message-ID: <1157641100.4567.10.camel@localhost.localdomain>

I've been playing around with sed recently and am surprised by why and
how this line doesn't accomplish the task I intended, which would be
capitalizing the first lower case letter in each line:

sed 's/[[:lower:]]/[[:upper:]]/' testing.txt

The file "testing.txt" contains this line:
THIS IS a lower case letter.

The result of the command is:
THIS IS [[:upper:]] lower case letter.

So it seems to use the POSIX character class to find the first lower
case letter, but then instead of replacing it with a matching upper case
letter, it inserts the string "[[:upper:]]"

Two questions:
Any idea why sed works like this?
Other solutions?

Thanks,

David

My current machine has re-booted itself twice in the last 24-48 hours. I 
was sitting at my desk the first time it happened, and watched it 
respond the way it would if I had hit the hardware reset button on the 
front of the box. The second time was overnight last night.

Any recommendations on what to test to see if this is a relatively 
simple fix?

The machine is an older Micron: Pentium II, 192MB RAM.

After the first time, SuSE 9.1 booted correctly and all programs that I 
normally use started correctly. After the second time, my network card 
configuration was hosed (this has happened before), and when I started 
Thunderbird and Firefox, neither opened with my usual profile, but 
instead wanted me to select one. Realizing that there was a network 
issue, I started YaST, made it look like I had edited the Ethernet card 
configuration, and clicked on "finish". In the past, that's all I needed 
to do, but this time that wasn't enough -- I still could not see outside 
this machine. However, a re-boot cleared whatever was confused, and I'm 
back to normal, as far as I can tell. At least Thunderbird and Firefox 
started with my profile.

you can get memtest86 is on a gentoo livecd.

distro, and see if the problem still occurs.  This would eliminate your
suse load and the hard drive from the equation.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Thu Sep  7 09:26:33 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Thu, 7 Sep 2006 09:26:33 -0700

On Thu, Sep 07, 2006 at 09:15:43AM -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> My current machine has re-booted itself twice in the last 24-48 hours. I 
> was sitting at my desk the first time it happened, and watched it 
> respond the way it would if I had hit the hardware reset button on the 
> front of the box. The second time was overnight last night.

Most likely cause: the CPU or power-supply fan's stopped spinning.

Cheap used replacements available at FreeGeek for the price of a nice
cup of coffee.  Entire replacement computers with higher specs available
there for the price of a nice meal.

50% off if you put in a few hours as a volunteer.

Less likely cause: corrosion in the RAM or CPU contacts.  Remove, dust
and reinstall RAM and CPU(s).

Good luck,
  Aaron

> 
> Notice that it's substituting a "u" for and "l" here...

After perusing the docs, it looks like SED doesn't support nice regexes
for y//.  The examples use e.g. y/abcdef/ABCDEF/ instead.  The tr shell
command does, though.  

   aaron at abu:~$ tr [[:lower:]] [[:upper:]] < testing.txt
   THIS IS A LOWER CASE LETTER.

Have fun,
  Aaron

>

Hardware is fun! Here are the bits I've been using the last couple years and 
liking a lot:

-Antec Sonata case. love them. They're big, but you can't beat their cooling, 
quietness, and ease of access
-Thermaltake power supplies are very good and nicely-priced. Of course PC 
Power & Cooling are the top-of-the-line, if you can afford them.
-SATA hard drive or drives
-Crucial and Kingston RAM are affordable and good-quality
-Don't go cheap on the motherboard because this will hurt you over the long 
run. Check the reviews on Newegg.com and Anandtech. Tom's Hardware used to be 
helpful, but who has the time or patience to click through twenty ad-laden 
pages just to read a single article?
-AMD processors. Semprons are great buys, though I suppose if you can find a 
deal on an Athlon that would be a good choice. AMD 64 has native 32-bit 
support, so you can use either 32- or 64-bit Linux. It looks like socket AM2 
is the new kewl socket, and 754 is being phased out

Gotchas to look out for:

-RAM comes in all kinds of speeds now. You must get the correct speed
-PCI comes in multiple flavors:
	
	3.3 volt
	5 volt
	PCI-Express

If you have the budget, go for PCI-E. This uses dedicated buses instead of a 
shared bus for all devices, so it's very fast, and it's backwards-compatible, 
so you can still use your old PCI adapters.
 
Voltage incompatibility can bite you. 5 volt is the old stuff, 3.3 is new. 
Some mobos support both. 

Start your research at Newegg.com. Their prices are great, lots of customer 
reviews, and their shipping is almost instant. It's a good place to do your 
homework before talking to a local vendor.

?and sysadmin's guide! http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Sep  7 10:05:28 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 7 Sep 2006 10:05:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Sed and POSIX character class question
In-Reply-To: <1157641100.4567.10.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1157641100.4567.10.camel@localhost.localdomain>
Message-ID: <200609071005.29240.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from David Pool
# on Thursday 07 September 2006 07:58 am:

>I've been playing around with sed recently and am surprised by why and
>how this line doesn't accomplish the task I intended, which would be
>capitalizing the first lower case letter in each line:
>
>sed 's/[[:lower:]]/[[:upper:]]/' testing.txt

Is this history coursework or just curiosity?

I found this nugget in the manual:
  # check if converted file name is equal to original file name,
  # if it is, do not print nothing
  /^.*\/\(.*\)\n\1/b

Which is about half a line below:

  LOWER='abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz'
  UPPER='ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ'

I spent a few hours in the sed manual once and all I took away was:

  echo "foo
  bar
  baz" | sed -n '2{p;q}'

That was enough.  It might be interesting to see sed rebuilt with perl 
regular expressions, but Sed.pm isn't quite what I had in mind.  Just 
something that makes the other features of sed accessible for things 
like:

  echo "foo
  bar
  baz" | perl -e 'print +(<>)[1]'

(which is likely slower than the sed #{p;q} invocation)

And of course sed still suprises me on occasion.  Maybe the examples are 
just really bad.  After reading the perlre manpage, I decided to poke 
at sed from a different angle.

Did you want to capitalize the first letter?
  echo "foo" | sed 's/^\(.\)/\u\1/'

Or the first lowercase letter?
  sed 's/\([[:lower:]]\)/\u\1/'

Or
  sed 's/\(.*\)/\U\1\E/'

--Eric
-- 
"It is impossible to make anything foolproof because fools are so 
ingenious."
--Murphy's Second Corollary

    http://scratchcomputing.com

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Thu Sep  7 10:15:20 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Thu, 07 Sep 2006 10:15:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] New Hardware Recommendations 
In-Reply-To: Message from Carla Schroder <carla@bratgrrl.com> of "Thu,
	07 Sep 2006 09:49:44 PDT." <200609070949.44585.carla@bratgrrl.com> 
Message-ID: <20060907171525.D4A238F00@tinman.seitzassoc.com>

Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> wrote:

> -PCI comes in multiple flavors:
> 	
> 	3.3 volt
> 	5 volt
> 	PCI-Express
> 
> If you have the budget, go for PCI-E. This uses dedicated buses
> instead of a shared bus for all devices, so it's very fast, and it's
> backwards-compatible, so you can still use your old PCI adapters.
>  

PCI Express slots are not backwards compatible with any prior generation
stuff.  Perhaps you were thinking of PCI-X.

  http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PCI-X

With the advent of PCI-Express, PCI-X is pretty much dead.  I doubt you
will find many motherboards that support PCI-X, particularly at the
consumer level.

PCI-Express motherboards typically have bridge chips that support
standard PCI slots. This is how they get backwards compatibility.

galen

If you want to build a box, then Carla's recommendation to start at 
newegg.com is a great one.

If you're content to buy a machine that'll do your "everyday stuff," 
then a Dell Dimension 1100n for $300 isn't a bad start. It's got a 
Celeron D -- which doesn't sound like much, but here at work we've got 
a couple running Xen with multiple VMs and they work great. Dell cases 
are roomy and fairly quiet; it's easy to add/upgrade hardware.

I usually get the 1100n rather than the standard 1100. It comes with 
FreeDOS rather than XP Home, and I get a warm feeling whenever Dell 
mails me a hard copy of the GPL. :-)

> for y//.  The examples use e.g. y/abcdef/ABCDEF/ instead.  The tr shell
> command does, though.  
> 
>    aaron at abu:~$ tr [[:lower:]] [[:upper:]] < testing.txt
>    THIS IS A LOWER CASE LETTER.

Ok, that works so kudos :-) and to answer Eric Wilhelm, it's mostly just
curiosity. I'm working through trying to put together a review of
O'Reilly's new LPI Certificiation book:

http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/lpicertnut2/

and got intrigued by the half support of POSIX in my example. I couldn't
tell if it was sed or myself that wasn't processing correctly :-)

So far the book is a great single source of prep for the LPI. Although
at 961 pages, calling it part of the "In a nutshell" series seems to
stretch the definition of nutshell...

David

On Thu, 2006-09-07 at 10:41 -0700, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> On Thu, 7 Sep 2006, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 
> > I'd like a machine that has sufficient horsepower and memory to 

FWIW, I'm looking at new laptops, and am having a devil of a time
figuring out what laptops have VT enabled (Virtualization Technology -
which enables installation of unaltered OSes) - primarily used for Xen
(and I gather Parallels). Seems like HP disables VT on all of its
laptops with Core Duo / Core 2 Duo CPUs (even though Intel advertises
this feature on all but the lowest end of these CPUs). 

Dell business sales seems OK, they indicated that all of their systems
with T2400 CPUs and above have VT enabled (or enableable). So I go to a
local Dell Outlet store to check it out, and lo and behold, the salesman
there refuses to allow me to look at the BIOS menu, says he's not even
allowed to do it himself. What BS!

Found a VT enable on one of the Toshibas (U205-S5022) at CompUSA. Looked
sweet, a fairly affordable laptop about 4 lbs. Next step is a Fry's
outing to check some of their BIOS.

Thanks,
Mike

On Thu, Sep 07, 2006 at 09:44:54AM -0700, Aaron Burt wrote:
> 
> Or just get a Dell.  They're quiet and have good warranties.

They may have changed, but I have had two horrible experiences
personally with Dell, and three more from acquaintances and business
partners.  Some subset of what they sell may come from a division
of this very large company on that makes reliable goods and supports
it well.  But as recently as a year ago, I worked with a company that
had a Dell boneyard of desktop boxes and LCD displays - about 3 dead
units per month for a 15 person company.  There was a significant
impact on productivity as these damned things went flaky.

I could go on about the laptop that took 3 months to repair, with the
baldface lie count reaching a dozen, or the GIANT boneyard in the
back room of a small bay area support company, but you get the picture.
Dell is a big company, and they probably have good divisions along with
the bad ones I encountered;  if you have had good luck with one of the
divisions, you should identify the particular product so people can
focus on that..

Keith

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Thu Sep  7 15:17:40 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Thu, 7 Sep 2006 16:17:40 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] New Hardware Recommendations (Nix Dell)
In-Reply-To: <20060907150251.GB28648@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <450047E0.90505@comcast.net> <20060907164454.GO30417@syrinxpc.com>
	<20060907150251.GB28648@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609071608590.20777@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I'd like to add a dell story or two:

A guy I work with had a dell workstation.  One day the fan started
racing out of control.  Turned out there were a dozen or so large
capacitors on the motherboard that had melted down.

Another guy at a previous place of work had a dell latitude laptop which
tanked.  It took a year for dell to send a working replacement.  In the
process, they sent him another latitude that also tanked.  Many lies
involved there too.

I had a dell inspiron 8200 laptop.  I got 2 and a half years out of it
before the motherboard tanked.  That's $600 a year for the use of a
laptop - too much money in my opinion.

Building your own machine is the way to go.  If the thing is going to
die, it shouldn't cost you a house payment.  If you need a portable
unit, consider building a mini-itx box in a briefcase.  In fact, stick 2
boards in there and cluster them together.  That's my plan.

Carlos Konstanski

I, too, have had recent disappointing hardware experiences with Dell 
systems, the Dimension line in particular.  My company purchased about 8 
systems in the course of a year, 3 of which have had hard drive problems 
within the first 4 months.  Still, contacting Dell for replacement 
hardware was not difficult and relatively speedy.  If you go with Dell, 
I would recommend shelling out a few more for the Optiplex system line 
which has a better hardware track record from my experience.

# from Keith Lofstrom
# on Thursday 07 September 2006 08:02 am:

>if you have had good luck with one of the
>divisions, you should identify the particular product so people can
>focus on that..

Careful, the bad divisions might get word of that and try to make 
themselves look better by sabotaging the competition (like you said, 
it's a big company -- the probably don't even know they're on the same 
team.)

My Dill laptop was not too impressive.  Despite never having traveled 
much, there's a crack in the lid and a corner missing next to the 
hinge.  There was also a ghost in the keyboard at one point, but that 
might not have been Dill's fault.  Of course, my Sharp^WDull now has a 
C key which pops off if hit it just wrong and that's only after a year 
and a half (but maybe that's from hitting ctrl+c too often while trying 
to deal with the emperor linux kernel.)

As for workstations, "never buy a workstation from anyone" is probably a 
good rule of thumb.  There are probably a few good vendors out there 
(somewhere), but the mainstream vendors seem to think you'll be happy 
as long as it has a "pentium 9" sticker on the front even with doggy 
on-board video, half a power supply, a pittance of the slowest ram they 
could find, and a hard drive that runs about as fast as a couple of 
distracted scribe-wielding hamsters.

--Eric

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Sep  6 11:13:01 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Unknown)
Date: Wed, 06 Sep 2006 11:13:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Samba/Windows 98/XP browse protocol
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051742430.21764@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <44FD77AF.1070204@alcpress.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609051742430.21764@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <1157566382.3946.12.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

On Tue, 2006-09-05 at 17:56 -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> I don't recall seeing this behavior in the last mixed network I was in,
> which utilized a linux box as the master browser.  We used settings like
> these in smb.conf:
> 
>      os level = 99
>      preferred master = Yes
>      domain master = Yes
>      local master = Yes
>      enhanced browsing = Yes
>      wins support = Yes
> 
> Although looking at the official samba docs, it seems that they use:
> 
>      os level = 65
> 
> instead of 99.
> 
> I'm not seeing any definition of "enhanced browsing" at the moment, but
> I seem to recall it has something to do with older windows vs. newer
> windows.  In the network where these settings were used, both 98 and XP
> boxes could browse.
> 
> I used to spend many hours watching BROWSER packets in ethereal in our
> network of 100+ computers.  Every time something went wrong with
> browsing, it would be found in this manner, and it invariably was a
> rogue machine with el-crappo WINS settings.  We dished out WINS settings
> via DHCP, but if a windows box has hardcoded values, it will use those
> instead.  One such windows box will take down the whole ship.  You ought
> to look for machines that have bogus WINS server settings, or are set to
> broadcast unnecessarily.  Look for machines trying to take over as
> master browser.  There will be a tug-of-war between the real master
> browser and the rogue box.
> 
> I have since moved on to a new company, which is a windows shop all the
> way (how I got them to let me build my workstation with linux is still a
> mystery).  Therefore, I have no access to a mixed samba network any more
> (my home network is 100% linux, and no samba).  So I'm riding on pure
> memory here.  Hope I didn't steer you wrong.
> 
> Carlos Konstanski
> 
> On Tue, 5 Sep 2006, Ed Sawicki wrote:
> 
> > Date: Tue, 05 Sep 2006 06:12:15 -0700
> > From: Ed Sawicki <ed at alcpress.com>
> > To: plug at lists.pdxlinux.org
> > Subject: [PLUG] Samba/Windows 98/XP browse protocol
> > 
> > A customer had an entirely Windows 98 network until today.
> > When they added a Windows XP box, the Windows 98 machines
> > would no longer display anything in Network Neighborhood.
> > I assume that the browse protocol is different and/or the
> > XP box now wins browser elections. Can Samba service such
> > a mixed network?
> >
> > A related matter: When I installed a new server with the latest
> > Samba, the Windows 98 computers did not see Samba file shares -
> > this was before the XP box was connected.
> >
> > Ed

Question: Is Windows networking token based like token ring networks
are?  How does ReactOS do with samba?  I know ReactOS isn't out of
alpha yet, but it might offer a nice alternative to broken MS-Windows
systems eventually.  Concerning browser elections, why does Microsoft
networking require master browser, etc?  With all the Netscape IE
cases in the late 90's, why didn't the justice department force 
Microsoft to redesign Windows so that competitors can replace the
networking code outright including the TCP/IP stack?  
Apparently, IE upgrades had a tendency to break a DLL that Netscape
depended on.  I've never seen an outright ban of this particular
anti-competitive tactic.  If Microsoft wasn't an application 
software and operating system company, it'd be more limited in
it's ability to control middle ware.

ReactOS is at http://www.reactos.org/.

BTW:  Freedos 1.0 finally got released.  http://www.freedos.org/

These two efforts aren't a drop in replacement yet for Windows,
but perhaps that day is coming.

Regards,

Michael Robinson

From dave.houston at gmail.com  Fri Sep  8 19:52:11 2006
From: dave.houston at gmail.com (Dave Houston)
Date: Fri, 08 Sep 2006 19:52:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Local Web Hosting
Message-ID: <1157770332.6938.9.camel@localhost.localdomain>

The $job has asked I find a local web host (local is very important)
that is both "good" and will have "good customer service".  

What's needed:
- Web hosting, with FrontPage extensions, FTP, and shell (SSH/Telnet)
access.  The big-three (Perl, PHP, and SSI) are implied requirements.  
- Email hosting, accessible either by POP3 or IMAP (preferred), or both.

It's important that the host have a pretty dang good uptime (ie, not
taken down every weekend for "maintenance" or some such) and be
reasonably priced.  Ideally, they'd have a fairly patient technical
support staff as well -- I may not always be around.

Suggestions?

A quick google found only a couple, Aracnet(SpiritOne) and Canvass
Dreams.
-- 
Dave Houston <dave.houston at gmail.com>

On Thu, 2006-09-07 at 18:34 -0700, Nathaniel Nuss wrote:
> On Thu, Sep 07, 2006 at 07:58:19AM -0700, David Pool wrote:
> > I've been playing around with sed recently and am surprised by why and
> > how this line doesn't accomplish the task I intended, which would be
> > capitalizing the first lower case letter in each line:
> > 
> > sed 's/[[:lower:]]/[[:upper:]]/' testing.txt
> 
> How about:
> 
> sed 's/[[:lower:]]/\u&/'

I did:
perl -i.bak -pe's/^(.*?)([a-z])(.*)/$1\u$2$3/' inputfile

And was pretty verbose with the regexp, just to be on the safe side.

From kens at cad2cam.com  Sat Sep  9 09:17:08 2006
From: kens at cad2cam.com (Kenneth G. Stephens)
Date: Sat, 09 Sep 2006 09:17:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Evolution Solution
Message-ID: <1157818628.9240.7.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>

Every once in a while, my Evolution Email Reader just croaks.  The inbox
gets out of synch and starts filling up.  I use to go into the
~/.evolution/mail/local and delete the indexes and things would be fine
for a while.  Then one day last week this did not work.  I started
looking around again and found the
~/.evolution/mail/spool/var/spool/mail/<username> directory which had
INBOX.cmeta, INBOX.ibex.index, and INBOX.ibex.index.data files and
some .%23evolution%2f/Junk.meta and .%23evolution%2fTrash.cmeta files.
Deleting these files, then restarting evolution got evolution back in
synch.

HTH someone.

Ken
CAD 2 CAM

>>  for a while.  Then one day last week this did not work.  I started
>>  looking around again and found the
>>  ~/.evolution/mail/spool/var/spool/mail/<username> directory which had
>>  INBOX.cmeta, INBOX.ibex.index, and INBOX.ibex.index.data files and
>>  some .%23evolution%2f/Junk.meta and .%23evolution%2fTrash.cmeta files.
>>  Deleting these files, then restarting evolution got evolution back in
>>  synch.
>>
>>  HTH someone.

This is an answer if you do POP, but not if you are an IMAP or Exchange
client.  Here's all the folders I have that are siblings to the local/
folder:

drwxr-xr-x 2 ckonstanski ckonstanski 9744 Sep  9 11:57 config
drwx------ 3 ckonstanski ckonstanski  104 Dec 18  2005 exchange
drwx------ 5 ckonstanski ckonstanski  208 Jan 28  2006 imap
drwxr-xr-x 3 ckonstanski ckonstanski 1296 Sep  9 11:57 local
drwx------ 3 ckonstanski ckonstanski   96 Dec 18  2005 pop
-rw------- 1 ckonstanski ckonstanski   68 Sep  9 11:57 searches.xml
drwxr-xr-x 2 ckonstanski ckonstanski   80 Sep  7 15:10 vfolder
drwxr-xr-x 2 ckonstanski ckonstanski 3376 Sep  8 12:32 views

One cannot rely on IMAP and Exchange emails all getting cached locally.

Carlos Konstanski

From russj at dimstar.net  Sat Sep  9 13:37:41 2006
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Sat, 09 Sep 2006 13:37:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] CRT dark...
In-Reply-To: <1157069485.44f77aade3556@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1157069485.44f77aade3556@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <45032615.5080407@dimstar.net>

plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> I have a 19" Viewsonic that has gotten dark.  I like it because it is compatible
> with Linux, it can acheive 1600x1280 max resolution.
I've not heard of a monitor that won't work with Linux. Yes, there are 
edge cases, but this doesn't sound like one of them.

I've heard of monitors that won't do the resolution you are asking for, 
but that's not the fault of Linux. That's the hardware.

What I've found is that monitors are less expensive to acquire than to fix.

Even this 20.1" widescreen LCD I'm looking at right now was less than 
$400, does 1680 X 1050, and has a fantastic picture. It works in every 
OS I've thrown at it, and I test a lot of them.

A 19" CRT monitor that supports 1600x1280 resolution should be less than 
$200 brand new. That's why it's hard to find repair shops. They can't 
stay in business fixing 2 or 3 monitors a year, when buying a new 
monitor is easier and faster.

Russ

From eehouse at eehouse.org  Mon Sep 11 09:09:38 2006
From: eehouse at eehouse.org (eehouse)
Date: Mon, 11 Sep 2006 16:09:38 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] How to confirm OSS support before hardward purchase?
Message-ID: <20060911160938.O19792@eehouse.org>

I'm shopping for a new machine to run Debian or Ubuntu fulltime.  So
I'm looking for a way to confirm, ideally prior to purchase, that a
machine is fully compatible with free software.

I used to think just booting it off an Ubuntu Live CD would be enough:
if video and sound work it's cool.  But my work-issue Dell laptop runs
Ubuntu, and I recently discovered Ubuntu installed proprietary
binary-only video drivers for its nvidia graphics subsystem.
Binary-only is ok for work, but not for a home machine.  So I need
another strategy.

(Maybe.  A related question is whether it's possible to run nvidia or
ATI chips, perhaps without using all their features, using open-source
drivers.  I need to run Gimp and emacs on this machine, not Doom.)

There's also the issue, mentioned here recently, of testing whether an
Intel-based box has "Virtualization Technology" disabled.  That's
never written up in company specs, and sales droids don't know what it
is.

How do folks on this list verify that a machine is fully open-source
friendly?  Short if taking advantage of Fry's 14-day return policy, I
mean. :-)

Thanks,

--Eric

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon Sep 11 09:35:55 2006
From: alan at clueserver.org (alan)
Date: Mon, 11 Sep 2006 09:35:55 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] How to confirm OSS support before hardward purchase?
In-Reply-To: <20060911160938.O19792@eehouse.org>
References: <20060911160938.O19792@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609110929090.9132@blackbox.fnordora.org>

On Mon, 11 Sep 2006, eehouse wrote:

> I'm shopping for a new machine to run Debian or Ubuntu fulltime.  So
> I'm looking for a way to confirm, ideally prior to purchase, that a
> machine is fully compatible with free software.
>
> I used to think just booting it off an Ubuntu Live CD would be enough:
> if video and sound work it's cool.  But my work-issue Dell laptop runs
> Ubuntu, and I recently discovered Ubuntu installed proprietary
> binary-only video drivers for its nvidia graphics subsystem.
> Binary-only is ok for work, but not for a home machine.  So I need
> another strategy.
>
> (Maybe.  A related question is whether it's possible to run nvidia or
> ATI chips, perhaps without using all their features, using open-source
> drivers.  I need to run Gimp and emacs on this machine, not Doom.)

The ATI open source drivers work fairly well, the nVIDIA open source 
drivers less so.  The part you want is not just the OpenGL, but the 
hardware acceleration.  You will want to avoid the real high end ATI 
chipsets though, because some are not supported.

The Intel video chipsets are also well supported with xorg 7.1.

The other closed source place you might get bit is if you need a modem. 
Most of the "WinModems" are pretty hard to get working under Linux due to 
proprietary bits.

Wireless cards are sometimes a pain.  Depends on the chipset.  The MadWifi 
drivers cover a number that are not supported out of the box.

One way to figure out what you are in for is to boot off a knopix disc and 
look at /sbin/lspci .

> There's also the issue, mentioned here recently, of testing whether an
> Intel-based box has "Virtualization Technology" disabled.  That's
> never written up in company specs, and sales droids don't know what it
> is.

I am not certain how you would check that other than booting off a very 
recient kernel and checking dmesg output.

> How do folks on this list verify that a machine is fully open-source
> friendly?  Short if taking advantage of Fry's 14-day return policy, I
> mean. :-)

"Fry's" is a descriptive verb.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Sep 11 10:18:08 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 11 Sep 2006 10:18:08 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] How to confirm OSS support before hardward purchase?
In-Reply-To: <20060911160938.O19792@eehouse.org>
References: <20060911160938.O19792@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0609111009260.5164@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 11 Sep 2006, eehouse wrote:

> There's also the issue, mentioned here recently, of testing whether 
> an Intel-based box has "Virtualization Technology" disabled.  
> That's never written up in company specs, and sales droids don't 
> know what it is.

In /proc/cpuinfo, the 'flags' section should report 'vmx' if ia32 VT 
is enabled.

I haven't, however, checked to see if enabling or disabling VT in BIOS 
toggles the presence of the vmx flag.

My experience is that Dell ships with VT disabled, at least on Pentium 
D chips. I don't know about other hardware/BIOS makers.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

There is support for the broadcom chipsets, you just need to have the
firmware.

In Fedora Core 5 extras there is a program for removing the firmware bits
from the Windows drivers.   It is called " bcm43xx-fwcutter".  I have used
it and it has worked pretty well.

	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609110929090.9132@blackbox.fnordora.org>
Message-ID: <1158006485.6676.2.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Mon, 2006-09-11 at 09:35 -0700, alan wrote:

> The ATI open source drivers work fairly well, the nVIDIA open source 
> drivers less so.  The part you want is not just the OpenGL, but the 
> hardware acceleration.  You will want to avoid the real high end ATI 
> chipsets though, because some are not supported.

To follow up on that -- the "ati" driver (the free one) supports just
about every ATI card there is, though, any ATI card with a R300 core (I
think the Radeon 9500 and higher) does not support Direct Rendering.
Work is being done, as I understand, to bring that support, but it's
slow and buggy, at best. the binary "fglrx" driver from ATI supports
every card they have.  Also, both ATI drivers fail to support the
COMPOSITE extension.

-- 
Dave Houston <dave.houston at gmail.com>

>>>>> "alan" == alan  <alan at clueserver.org> writes:

alan> Wireless cards are sometimes a pain.  Depends on the chipset.
alan> The MadWifi drivers cover a number that are not supported out of
alan> the box.

MadWifi drivers support Atheros chipset radios, except USB, which it
doesn't support.  

Personal Telco uses madwifi a lot on the Mississippi Grant Project,
and have a love/hate relationship with it.  By and large, it works
pretty well but has its faults.  The developers suck on the
manufacturer's toes a little too much for my tastes, they are totally
dependent on Atheros to release a binary HAL to let it work,
madwifi-ng has had a tendency to kernel panic if you ifdown/ifup too
much, etc.

I use the older madwifi on my laptop (with an atheros a/b/g card) and
it is pretty stable.

From danielmyoung at gmail.com  Mon Sep 11 18:18:19 2006

Subject: [PLUG] PCMCIA CF / SD card reader suggestions?
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80609110904h7e5c7771l8685a78bba4167c0@mail.gmail.com>
References: <c834ec80609110904h7e5c7771l8685a78bba4167c0@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0609111818r532fa45fhab5613ceb4638bce@mail.gmail.com>

In my experience, you won't find a PCMCIA card w/ both CF and SD,
given the size of CF.

I got an CF to PCMCIA for my wife and she leaves it in all the time.
Since it's flush w/ the computer, you have to pop it out to get the CF
card back out. They are extremely simple (just an "unpowered plug
adapter" according to wikipedia).

Dunno about SD -> PCMCIA. If you could find a Type 2, you could stack
them in your PCMCIA slots.

--
Dan

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sat Sep  9 15:32:44 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Sat,  9 Sep 2006 15:32:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] CRT dark...
In-Reply-To: <45032615.5080407@dimstar.net>
References: <1157069485.44f77aade3556@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<45032615.5080407@dimstar.net>
Message-ID: <1157841164.4503410c902fb@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting Russ Johnson <russj at dimstar.net>:

> plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> > I have a 19" Viewsonic that has gotten dark.  I like it because it is
> compatible
> > with Linux, it can acheive 1600x1280 max resolution.
> I've not heard of a monitor that won't work with Linux. Yes, there are 
> edge cases, but this doesn't sound like one of them.
> 
> I've heard of monitors that won't do the resolution you are asking for, 
> but that's not the fault of Linux. That's the hardware.
> 
> What I've found is that monitors are less expensive to acquire than to fix.
> 
> Even this 20.1" widescreen LCD I'm looking at right now was less than 
> $400, does 1680 X 1050, and has a fantastic picture. It works in every 
> OS I've thrown at it, and I test a lot of them.
> 
> A 19" CRT monitor that supports 1600x1280 resolution should be less than 
> $200 brand new. That's why it's hard to find repair shops. They can't 
> stay in business fixing 2 or 3 monitors a year, when buying a new 
> monitor is easier and faster.
> 
> Russ



First off, who is to say that monitors can't be fixed profitably?  I suppose
it's okay for people to simply throw electronic equipment in the dump if it
costs money to do the right thing.  It's easy to produce disposable electronics,
but it is not environmentally friendly.  I for one, like others I'm sure, 
want to be able to use what I know works.  Name one LCD panel or CRT
manufacturer anywhere that will let the buyer know their display is Linux
compatible up front.  It is safe to assume, for the most part, that a standard
monitor will work with Linux.  That which is valid today may not be valid 
in the future.

With a company like HP trying to "plug intellectual property leaks," 
it seems quite clear that companies want to have and charge the public 
for use of proprietary closed technology.  Does anyone think these companies
care which part of the hardware or software in a person's computer is
proprietary?  An attitude that the public shouldn't have access to 
technologies they understand completely which will serve their needs 
fully seems very much alive.  Since when is it a good move for HP's top exec 
to attempt to keep her position after being caught entrapping her own 
employees via illegal wire taps?  I used to think that HP was an OSS 
friendly company.  Anymore, I question the company's real motivation 
with this recent scandal.  HP generally isn't a monitor manufacturer, 
but it's the sorta attitude this scandal has revealed which suggests 
to me that their is room for future compatibility problems.

My monitor, though dark, is what I have right now.  I'm low on funds, it's a
shame if I have no money to dispose of it that it will be difficult to do the
environmentally right thing.  Monitors aren't the only problem, how many 
circuit boards contain lead solder?  Disposable digital cameras have circuitry
in them, what is the impact on the environment from these being tossed into
our landfills?  Will we poison vital groundwater sources by throwing consumer
electronics into our landfills?  If people have to spend their own money to do
the right thing with worn out electronic devices that aren't worth fixing, I
seriously that the majority will.

Michael Robinson

Turns out that in SBCL, it's as easy as:

(sb-debug:backtrace-as-list)

The file util.lisp in the TBNL source has a series of (get-backtrace)
functions (one for each flavor of lisp) that helped a lot.  Thanks.

Carlos Konstanski

On Mon, 2006-09-11 at 14:37 -0700, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> On Mon, 11 Sep 2006, Ted Kubaska wrote:
> 
> > This VT.. is this the so-called Van der Pool technology?
> 
> Sorry, I don't know the Intel code names. It's the virtualization 
> stuff (like AMD's Pacifica), that allows hypervisors to run unmodified 
> x86 operating systems in lightweight virtual machines., 

Right, most closely associated with Xen. Without VT, Xen only works with
"paravirtualized" (i.e. Xen modified) OSes. The Xen developers have
reportedly modified Win2003 Server to work in a paravirtualized
environment, but can't release it w/o MS permission.

is looking for, I think.

  alias scsi_hostadapter adp94xx
  alias eth0 e1000
  alias eth1 e1000
  alias eth2 e1000

Strangely, the adp94xx and eth0 show up on the same IRQ, and the BIOS
won't allow the Adaptec card's interrupt to be forced.
