From daniel at digitree.org  Mon Sep 25 00:54:09 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 00:54:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Modifying LC_TIME
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609242018400.4375@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609241655080.3745@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<45172706.80900@digitree.org>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609241810230.3745@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<451744AC.1090902@digitree.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609242018400.4375@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <45178B21.1030304@digitree.org>

Rich,

Rich Shepard wrote:
> week-1stday=19971201
> 
>   If I change the week-1stday -- somehow -- to 19971130 then the week will
> start on Sunday. Also, I could change the first_workday to =2 and that 
> would

19971201 -> 1997/12/01 (YYYY/MM/DD) = Monday
19971130 -> 1997/11/30 (YYYY/MM/DD) = Sunday

This problem that you describe appears to have been widely reported as a 
bug in GNU C library's locale.
http://sourceware.org/bugzilla/show_bug.cgi?id=2371

I'm not sure what version of the glibc library you're running, but 
you're probably stuck with one of the versions that has the bug.  The 
version I'm using, 2.4.11 doesn't seem to be affected by this problem 
and my week-1stday entry appears as 19971130...so the problem has been 
fixed at some point.

I only see the following options for you:
1. upgrade glibc (ugly and probably not worth it!)

2. change to a different locale for the LC_TIME variable, such as 
en_US.UTF-8 and see if that provides a different value for week-1stday. 
  The following code automates testing every single locale on the system 
that matches your desired LC_TIME settings:
#!/bin/bash
# finds other locales that match the desired LC_TIME settings
export LC_TIME="en_US"
locale -k LC_TIME | sed s/19971201/19971130/ > locale.old
for i in `locale -a`; do
    export LC_TIME="$i"
    locale -k LC_TIME > locale.new
    (diff --brief locale.old locale.new > /dev/null) \
      && echo "match found...$i"
done

3. use localedef.  I wrote a bash script specifically to do this for 
you!  Well, for anyone else who comes across the bug in the future and 
for myself.  I wanted to be able to change the starting day of the week 
for the en_AU locale to be on a Monday (which for some reason it isn't). 
  I can imagine that several months in the future when I 
reinstall/upgrade my distro that I'll forget to copy over the new 
locales (or they won't work), and I'll have forgotten how to fix things 
again.  I've tested the script on my system and it seemed to work. 
There is nothing destructive and it can be run as non-root.  The 
defaults on the configuration variables are already set for your bug; 
just run the script and follow the "further instructions".  Have a look 
here:
http://projects.digitree.org/miscellaneous/wiki/locale_week_fix

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From daniel at digitree.org  Mon Sep 25 03:00:50 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 03:00:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] grub and fdisk problems mean what?
In-Reply-To: <20060925055427.A34273@eehouse.org>
References: <20060925055427.A34273@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <4517A8D2.2050802@digitree.org>

Eric,

I hope you make backups (see more below).


eehouse wrote:
> Disk /dev/hda: 40.0 GB, 40016019456 bytes
> 255 heads, 63 sectors/track, 4865 cylinders
> Units = cylinders of 16065 * 512 = 8225280 bytes
> 
>    Device Boot      Start         End      Blocks   Id  System
> /dev/hda1               1           1        8001    1  FAT12
> 
> If I go ahead and mount /dev/hda1 (allowing mount to determine the
> fs type itself) things look ok:

It actually looks like you've hosed (overwritten) your partition table 
somehow.  You said there should be 3 partitions.  There is only one 
partition listed and that maxed out at 8 gigabytes.

If I had to guess about what caused the problem, it might be that you 
(or a script) accidentally selected or typed /dev/hda instead of 
/dev/sda1 when repartitioning/reformatting an external USB drive of some 
description.  Either that or you managed to overwrite your /dev/hda 
partition table with one from an 8 gig external drive.  I highly doubt 
you would've manually repartitioned the hard disk and chose the 
partition layout it's set at.  The final possibility is that part of 
your hard disk just corrupted itself, but if that was the case then 
fdisk would most likely report back that the partition table was corrupt 
and ask you to reinitialize it.


> ubuntu at ubuntu:~$ mount | grep hda1
> /dev/hda1 on /mnt type ext2 (rw)

If /dev/hda1 mounted then it was probably a Linux filesystem.  Your 
partition table above should've read "Linux" under the System column if so.


> And the size and amount of stuff on there look about right:
> But when I try to look at any of the files I get errors:

This is because your partition's start and end blocks are wrong.  Your 
partition was large enough that it was able to read enough of the inodes 
to display the filesystem but probably not enough for the rest of your 
files...not completely sure about how the ext2 filesystem works under 
these circumstances, but you're possibly in big trouble if you write 
anything back to the hard disk (especially the partition was mounted 
read-write above).  If you mount the partition in the future, please 
consider using mount's '-o ro' argument.


> ubuntu at ubuntu:/mnt$ ls /mnt/boot
> ls: /mnt/boot: Input/output error

This probably occurred because the inodes that Linux was looking for 
couldn't be found because they were outside of the partition's geomentry 
(start and end blocks).


> In addition, I can't mount any of the other partitions:

You can't mount any of the other partitions because Linux doesn't know 
where to find the start and end of each partition since they're not 
listed in your partition table (above).


> I don't know much about udev, which this cd is using, but I gather
> that the device files being missing (and they are) could just mean
> that udev didn't notice the partitions (rather than that they really
> don't exist.)

It's not a problem with udev unfortunately.


> Oh, and looking through the logs I found a suggestion that I run
> e2fsck:

I'd be extremely careful and NOT run e2fsck at this point.


> Can anybody suggest what might be going on here, and maybe how to go
> about fixing it?  Is this a situation I could have gotten into by
> installing a new kernel?  Or did my superblock get trashed sometime
> over the 40 days since I last re-booted?

There are some tools you can get that will search your hard disk for 
partition boundaries and try to rebuild any partition tables you might 
have.  Have a look at the following program:
http://www.cgsecurity.org/wiki/TestDisk

The TestDisk software is included on Knoppix and a few other live CDs. 
I'm not sure about Ubuntu's live CD though:
http://www.cgsecurity.org/wiki/TestDisk_Livecd

If the above doesn't work and if you can find a way to get into your 
/dev/hda1's /boot/grub/ directory (which you've already demonstrated 
above that you probably can't), there is a file called 'stage1'.  That 
should be a copy of your MBR/primary partition table.  If you can 
somehow manage to read that file, you should be able to execute 
something similar to:
dd bs=1 count=512 if=/mnt/boot/grub/stage1 of=/dev/hda

Provided the stage1 file is up to date, you *might* have some luck.  It 
probably won't kill things any more than they are.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From pmvw at outwardfocus.net  Mon Sep 25 04:08:11 2006
From: pmvw at outwardfocus.net (Piet van Weel)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 03:08:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] grub and fdisk problems mean what?
In-Reply-To: <20060925055427.A34273@eehouse.org>
References: <20060925055427.A34273@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <4517B89B.2020908@outwardfocus.net>

Interesting problem. Judging from what you are seeing and have told us I 
hope your backups are in order. *Just in case*

The superblock probably got corrupted, but have faith all is not lost. *yet*

To see how to fix this we need to take the view that your hard drive is 
like a library. Each spot on your hard drive is like a book on the shelf 
in this library.  Fortunately for the library it has several areas; 
unfortunately, they are cryptically named hda1, hda2, and hda3. Down at 
the front reference desk, the superblock, there is a reference area 
telling you which area to look in.

In your case someone erased or altered your reference area!! The books 
are still there, and so are the areas. So we need to change it back to 
what it was!!

You mentioned in your e-mail that there were supposed to be three 
partitions on your system.
  hda1 is 15 Gig (ext2)
  hda2 is 10 Gig (fat-32)
  hda3 is 15 Gig (ext2)

So in order to fix your reference area, I would boot with a live CD. 
This will enable you to alter your boot record without having the hard 
drive being the primary boot device.

Then I would bring up fdisk and delete hda1. After that I would create a 
new partition hda1 of 15 gigs, hda2 at 10 gigs, and hda3 at 15 gigs. 
Then I would change the types on those three partitions to match their 
respective formats. Remember to set the boot flag and write it to disk. 
Then quit out of fdisk.

Because you didn't boot with the hard drive you can go directly to 
mounting the hard drive without a reboot. :) At this point I would go 
through and mount hda1 and run a e2fsck on it.

* WARNING * WARNING *
Only run it to scan the partition, not to fix the partition. Once it 
gets the ability to write to the partition, it has the potential to ruin 
other data if the partition information was not correct.

If everything you said about the hard drive allocation was correct, it 
should come back like a champ. :) Of course once it is recovered and 
you're assured that all three partitions are good-to-go; then I would 
definitely make a backup of your hard drive.

Personally, I have done this method before and it worked. *thankfully*

I would be interested in hearing what the rest of PLUG has to say about 
this method.

Piet






From daniel at digitree.org  Mon Sep 25 04:21:33 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 04:21:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] grub and fdisk problems mean what?
In-Reply-To: <4517B89B.2020908@outwardfocus.net>
References: <20060925055427.A34273@eehouse.org>
	<4517B89B.2020908@outwardfocus.net>
Message-ID: <4517BBBD.2000702@digitree.org>

Piet van Weel wrote:
> Then I would bring up fdisk and delete hda1. After that I would create a 
> new partition hda1 of 15 gigs, hda2 at 10 gigs, and hda3 at 15 gigs. 
> Then I would change the types on those three partitions to match their 
> respective formats. Remember to set the boot flag and write it to disk. 
> Then quit out of fdisk.

Unfortunately Eric would have to get the block boundaries exactly the 
same as they were before.  If they're off by one, it could lead to 
corruption.  For someone who originally created the partitions using 
fdisk and is very sure that they just went '+15G' or similar, it would 
be feasible to use your approach.  For someone who didn't manually set 
up everything by hand in fdisk, it's probably too risky.  It didn't seem 
like Eric has had an awful lot of experience with partitioning in fdisk 
so I would probably rule out this approach for him unless other methods 
fail.

I admit that since his partition table is already stuffed, your approach 
probably won't make things worse...but is probably too high of a risk to 
ever consider mounting anything as read-write mode or even trying to 
boot the system.  I could see your approach possibly allowing Eric to 
gain enough access to the rest of ext2/ext3 filesystem inodes and maybe 
then being able to get to the /boot/grub/stage1 file to get the MBR 
records from that...but again in read-only mode.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Sep 25 06:19:06 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 06:19:06 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Modifying LC_TIME
In-Reply-To: <45178B21.1030304@digitree.org>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609241655080.3745@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<45172706.80900@digitree.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609241810230.3745@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<451744AC.1090902@digitree.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609242018400.4375@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<45178B21.1030304@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609250609210.27082@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 25 Sep 2006, Daniel Hedlund wrote:

> 19971201 -> 1997/12/01 (YYYY/MM/DD) = Monday
> 19971130 -> 1997/11/30 (YYYY/MM/DD) = Sunday

Daniel,

   Exactly.

> This problem that you describe appears to have been widely reported as a
> bug in GNU C library's locale.
> http://sourceware.org/bugzilla/show_bug.cgi?id=2371

   A-ha! I've experienced it only in gtk+2's calendar.

> I'm not sure what version of the glibc library you're running,

 	2.3.5

> ... but you're probably stuck with one of the versions that has the bug. 
> The version I'm using, 2.4.11 doesn't seem to be affected by this problem
> and my week-1stday entry appears as 19971130...so the problem has been
> fixed at some point.

   Well, fixed for those of us here in the US, anyway. :-) The fellow in GB
who wrote the gentoo fix wanted to go from a starting day of Sunday to that
of Monday.

> I only see the following options for you:
> 1. upgrade glibc (ugly and probably not worth it!)

   Not until Slackware-11.0 comes out ... Real Soon Now.

> 2. change to a different locale for the LC_TIME variable, such as
> en_US.UTF-8 and see if that provides a different value for week-1stday. 
> The following code automates testing every single locale on the system
> that matches your desired LC_TIME settings:
> #!/bin/bash
> # finds other locales that match the desired LC_TIME settings
> export LC_TIME="en_US"
> locale -k LC_TIME | sed s/19971201/19971130/ > locale.old
> for i in `locale -a`; do
>   export LC_TIME="$i"
>   locale -k LC_TIME > locale.new
>   (diff --brief locale.old locale.new > /dev/null) \
>     && echo "match found...$i"
> done

   Oooh, nice!

> 3. use localedef. I wrote a bash script specifically to do this for you!
> Well, for anyone else who comes across the bug in the future and for
> myself. I wanted to be able to change the starting day of the week for
> the en_AU locale to be on a Monday (which for some reason it isn't). I
> can imagine that several months in the future when I reinstall/upgrade my
> distro that I'll forget to copy over the new locales (or they won't work),
> and I'll have forgotten how to fix things again. I've tested the script
> on my system and it seemed to work. There is nothing destructive and it
> can be run as non-root. The defaults on the configuration variables are
> already set for your bug; just run the script and follow the "further
> instructions". Have a look here:
> http://projects.digitree.org/miscellaneous/wiki/locale_week_fix

   Thank you. That's teriffic and exceptionally helpful for a lot of folks.
You're right that a system upgrade will overwrite the changes, so it's wise
to keep the script handy.

Rich

so that I can use vmware-player.  Irritatingly enough, I believe I've 
tried to use yum in the past to grab the kernel sources only to give up 
when I couldn't figure out how to build an rpm.  Now after using mondo 
to backup and restore my system, none of the yum downloaded updates let 
alone the kernel sources are showing up.  Trying to yum download kernel, 
I get a nothing to do error.  Uge!!!!

Mondo broke selinux and I had to disable it, anyone have a clue as to why?

Is yum intelligent enough to detect that what it thinks it has in the package
cache isn't really there?  Is there any alternative to downloading Fedora
Core updates?  I want to flick messing with online updates for Fedora all
together, but the distro never seems to ship ready to do a whole lot without
any updates applied.  Between download corruption and having Fedora's own idea
of what has been downloaded corrupt, yum seems like a huge headache.

A problem with the yum philosophy, I want to maintain multiple Fedora
Core 5 installations and have both the source packages and binary
packages for all applied updates maintained on a centralized server.  
Yum seems to keep track of what updating has been done directly on 
the machine it is maintaining.  This obviously will duplicate work 
and it isn't very corruption resistant.  If FC5 hosta and hostb need
to pull down the same update to a site A through gateway foo, why can't
one of them download the update to foo and then both of them share a 
copy?  Is it possible to mirror the Fedora Core 5 repository in 
it's entirety?  If I drop $50 into a Fedora Core 5 Bible, I want to 
have a local copy of all the updates etc. when Fedora Core 6 comes out.  
I've tried CentOS, it hasn't worked well for me.  Slackware seems even 
worse about falling behind and update issues.

I wish the Fedora Core Bible came in an electronic format that is viewable 
using the free commonly installed tools available in Linux.  I don't have 
a lot of bookspace where I don't want to fill what I do have with one 
Fedora Bible after another.  Fedora turns over so fast and the current
repository will disappear shortly.  The redhat download servers in 
contrast go all the down to the 1.0 release.  I know that bandwidth is 
becoming a problem.  If Redhat hadn't thrown out 90% of it's potential 
paying customer base by trying to become in name a large customers only
supporter, redhat might be able to afford more bandwidth.

Michael C. Robinson

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon Sep 25 09:28:46 2006

> Before yum for redhat users, it was just rpm and that was it.
>
> I'm having trouble getting the kernel sources installed to Fedora Core 5
> so that I can use vmware-player.

You don't need kernel sources to make vmplayer work.  You need the package 
"kernel-devel" and the patch to the vmware kernel modules.

> Is yum intelligent enough to detect that what it thinks it has in the package
> cache isn't really there?

If the cache gets screwed up run "yum clean all".  It will clean out all 
the cached items.  (Sometimes you get things that are wrong in the cache 
because of a mirror that is in the middle of syncing at the time you hit 
it.)

> Is there any alternative to downloading Fedora
> Core updates?  I want to flick messing with online updates for Fedora all
> together, but the distro never seems to ship ready to do a whole lot without
> any updates applied.  Between download corruption and having Fedora's own idea
> of what has been downloaded corrupt, yum seems like a huge headache.

Yum is better than the options before it.

>
> A problem with the yum philosophy, I want to maintain multiple Fedora
> Core 5 installations and have both the source packages and binary
> packages for all applied updates maintained on a centralized server.

Then change the repositories to point to a local repository. 
/etc/yum.repos.d has the repository configs.


> Yum seems to keep track of what updating has been done directly on
> the machine it is maintaining.  This obviously will duplicate work
> and it isn't very corruption resistant.  If FC5 hosta and hostb need
> to pull down the same update to a site A through gateway foo, why can't
> one of them download the update to foo and then both of them share a
> copy?  Is it possible to mirror the Fedora Core 5 repository in
> it's entirety?  If I drop $50 into a Fedora Core 5 Bible, I want to
> have a local copy of all the updates etc. when Fedora Core 6 comes out.
> I've tried CentOS, it hasn't worked well for me.  Slackware seems even
> worse about falling behind and update issues.

Then you need to create a local repository and just rsync that repository.

As for corruption, the packages are signed and checksummed.  They are not 
going to install corrupted.

> I wish the Fedora Core Bible came in an electronic format that is viewable
> using the free commonly installed tools available in Linux.  I don't have
> a lot of bookspace where I don't want to fill what I do have with one
> Fedora Bible after another.  Fedora turns over so fast and the current
> repository will disappear shortly.  The redhat download servers in
> contrast go all the down to the 1.0 release.  I know that bandwidth is
> becoming a problem.  If Redhat hadn't thrown out 90% of it's potential
> paying customer base by trying to become in name a large customers only
> supporter, redhat might be able to afford more bandwidth.

There are tons of mirrors for getting Redhat packages.  Yum will pick 
whatever mirrors are available.

The advantage of yum is it will grab all the dependancies for the install 
without having to do it manually.

The Redhat bible is not needed to run Redhat or Fedora.  I think the last 
time I looked at one of those was around Redhat 4.2.

Once you understand how yum works, you can make it do what you want.  It 
is pretty flexable.

disk 3.5 years ago.  Testdisk will take several guesses and let me
choose, and I bet I can recognize it when I see it.

> If the above doesn't work and if you can find a way to get into your 
> /dev/hda1's /boot/grub/ directory (which you've already demonstrated 
> above that you probably can't), there is a file called 'stage1'.  That 
> should be a copy of your MBR/primary partition table.  If you can 
> somehow manage to read that file, you should be able to execute 
> something similar to:
> dd bs=1 count=512 if=/mnt/boot/grub/stage1 of=/dev/hda
>
> Provided the stage1 file is up to date, you *might* have some luck.  It 
> probably won't kill things any more than they are.

of the disk and running, say 15G.  Even if that's wrong, it'd give me
a much better chance of getting at that /boot/grub/stage1 file, after

On 9/24/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> http://www.grc.com/dos/winxp.htm
>
> How do you audit what can be done with RAW sockets on a Linux/Unix system?

You don't, unless you use something like SELinux.

> How are raw sockets secured on current Linux systems.

By only allowing root to open them.  This is no different than
Windows, except that Windows users always run as Administrator.

Steve has been crying about raw sockets for years, and yet the
internet is still alive and well.  There's enough zombies out there
that anyone who wants to perform a DDoS attack can do it whether they
have raw socket access or not.  As DDoS turns into big business
(extorsion) it would not take long for rootkits to include custom raw
socket code that hooks directly into NDIS, even if Microsoft didn't
provide full raw socket capabilities.  It is rather silly to assume
that Microsoft is responsible for all the DDoS zombies because of raw
sockets.  They are responsible for all the DDoS zombies because their
code keeps getting hacked and their security model has everyone using
their OS running as Administrator.

> With the Steve Gibson guy finding so many problems with Windows, why doesn't
> he get a clue and move to Linux or one of the BSD's?

Because he gets more fame and fortune for bashing the most widely deployed OS.

> Does anyone else think that $90 for spinrite is exorbitent?

Yes, but then again I have never lost critical data due to
filesystem/partition corruption.

> Is there a way under Linux to read smart information before one's hard disk
> dies?  This should be a free feature for both Windows and Linux systems in
> my opinion, not something you have to pay for.

smartd.

Jason


From daniel at digitree.org  Mon Sep 25 10:13:28 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 10:13:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] grub and fdisk problems mean what?
In-Reply-To: <20060925164338.F34273@eehouse.org>
References: <20060925155410.CB5A6472F@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<20060925164338.F34273@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <45180E38.60508@digitree.org>

eehouse wrote:
> I'm impressed!  A couple of weeks ago I tweaked the formatting of the
> 8M CF card that my router runs on.  (LEAF on a WRAP box.)  I probably
> ran the commands as root.  I was pretty stressed at the time (I'd
> killed the router and so had no 'net connection for troubleshooting),
> but would like to think I'm never that dumb. :-)

Oops.  Yeah, the 8 gigabytes should've been 8 megabytes.  It looks that 
the 8M CF card tweaking was what originally led to your problem.


>>> ubuntu at ubuntu:~$ mount | grep hda1
>>> /dev/hda1 on /mnt type ext2 (rw)
>> If /dev/hda1 mounted then it was probably a Linux filesystem.  Your 
>> partition table above should've read "Linux" under the System column if so.
> 
> Something confusing them, and each resolves the confusion differently?

While Linux doesn't really care all that much about the partition type 
listed in fdisk, other operating systems do.  Linux normally just tries 
to guess what a partition's type is.  Under Windows, if it was to detect 
a partition as a fat or ntfs-based partition in the partition table, it 
would probably show it as a drive letter and say that it's unformatted. 
  Ideally partition types commonly found under Linux (ext2, ext3, 
reiserfs, etc) should have its partition table entry set to 83 
("Linux").  Linux also commonly uses the type 83 ("Linux swap") to label 
swap-related partitions in the partition table.  You can get a listing 
of partition types available to choose from within fdisk by typing the 
letter 'l'.


> If I'm understanding correctly, I could modify the partition table to
> indicate that there's an ext2 partition, hda1, beginning at the start
> of the disk and running, say 15G.  Even if that's wrong, it'd give me
> a much better chance of getting at that /boot/grub/stage1 file, after
> which I could put the partition table back the way it should be with
> no harm done to the files themselves (everything but the partition
> table having been -ro all the time.)  Right?

In theory, I believe so.  In reality, I've never tried it to know if it 
helps.  I'm also not 100% sure if the 'stage1' file contains an exact 
replica of the partition tables you want.  I would assume it does since 
grub builds the stage1 file before writing it to the MBR, and thus 
partition table.  If you used grub or anything similar to play around 
with your 8MB card, then the stage1 file is probably wrong.  Otherwise 
it's worth a shot.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

> Before yum for redhat users, it was just rpm and that was it.

There are a couple of alternatives.

You might try apt, downloadable from atrpms.net. When I've usapted it in
the past, it's been faster and more reliable than yum, despite the
improvements they've made. 

Unfortunately, the maintainers behind apt for RPM distros are focusing
their energies on SmartPM, the "Smart Package Manager." I'm not up to
speed on the current state of SmartPM, but it's also available from
atrpms.net. 

For optimizing yum, there's a way to
point /etc/yum.repos.d/fedora-core.repo to a local share of the Fedora
Core install files - there are separate stanzas for binary and source
RPMs. Unfortunately, a recent yum update nuked my copy, just from
memory, I think the key is to include a line such as:

baseurl=file:///path/to/share/

where /path/to/share is a shared NFS directory where I mounted the FC5
install (and source) RPMs. Naturally, you'd comment out the other
baseurl and mirrorlist directives (the FC5 mirrorlist is really screwey,
it often points me to a mirror in some distant location such as New
Zealand).

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Mon Sep 25 10:46:21 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 10:46:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] I don't like yum...
In-Reply-To: <1159102971.451681fb29e46@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1159102971.451681fb29e46@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0609251046g74db0ab2i913795da0df733e2@mail.gmail.com>

On 9/24/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>
> Mondo broke selinux and I had to disable it, anyone have a clue as to why?
>

Probably because mondo does not save the selinux labels.  If the
labels aren't present, then selinux won't work correctly.  The
/sbin/fixfiles script will relabel the whole file system. This will be
done on boot if the /.relabel file exist.

> Is yum intelligent enough to detect that what it thinks it has in the package
> cache isn't really there?  Is there any alternative to downloading Fedora
> Core updates?  I want to flick messing with online updates for Fedora all
> together, but the distro never seems to ship ready to do a whole lot without
> any updates applied.  Between download corruption and having Fedora's own idea
> of what has been downloaded corrupt, yum seems like a huge headache.
>

Yum will detect corrupt and partially downloaded packages in the cache
and fetch them again.  The packages are checksummed in the metadata
and also signed.  Yum complain about files downloaded with the wrong
checksum.  And rpm won't install corrupted RPMs.

> A problem with the yum philosophy, I want to maintain multiple Fedora
> Core 5 installations and have both the source packages and binary
> packages for all applied updates maintained on a centralized server.
> Yum seems to keep track of what updating has been done directly on
> the machine it is maintaining.  This obviously will duplicate work
> and it isn't very corruption resistant.  If FC5 hosta and hostb need
> to pull down the same update to a site A through gateway foo, why can't
> one of them download the update to foo and then both of them share a
> copy?  Is it possible to mirror the Fedora Core 5 repository in
> it's entirety?  If I drop $50 into a Fedora Core 5 Bible, I want to
> have a local copy of all the updates etc. when Fedora Core 6 comes out.
> I've tried CentOS, it hasn't worked well for me.  Slackware seems even
> worse about falling behind and update issues.
>

Currently, you have three options:

1. Rsync local mirror of repository.  Then have every machine use the
local mirror.  I do this for the updates repository because it is
useful for installing packages.  And I can run it in the middle of the
night and have fast updates.

2. Proxy cache. For repositories which I don't want a complete mirror,
like development or extras, I have setup Apache to proxy requests to
the master server and cache the results.

3. Copy the files from the yum cache to a central location or to the
other machines.  New versions of yum will delete the files from the
cache when the operation is finished; there is a config option to turn
this off.

There have been some recent improvements.  The newer versions at
http://apt-rpm.org/ support multilib.  They also support the metadata
format used by yum.

> What does apt do that yum does not?  I have found them fairly similar.
>

Apt is smarter about not installing broken packages.  Yum will abort
the whole update if there is a conflict instead of installing the
packages which can be installed.  Apt used to be faster but recent
versions of rpm have improved the performance.  On the other hand, I
find the yum interface much easier and more complete. And the python
library is getting used in the installer and various graphical tools.

From daniel at digitree.org  Mon Sep 25 13:00:40 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 13:00:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: grub and fdisk problems mean what?
In-Reply-To: <20060925193623.J34273@eehouse.org>
References: <20060925190006.F323E4731@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<20060925193623.J34273@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <45183568.4000803@digitree.org>

eehouse wrote:
> No need to take that shot.  The problem is fixed.  testdisk worked
> beautifully.  I booted off the live cd, downloaded it to /tmp, and
> ran.  It first found what looked like the right partitions, but had
> the last two marked as "delete" and said they were corrupt when I
> tried to change that.  But it also mentioned that the number of heads
> per cylinder was set to 16 and recommended 255.  When I made that
> change it then found all four partitions, said they were fine, and let
> me write the new table out.  On reboot fsck was run, but didn't report
> finding any errors.  And now life is good again.

That's very good to hear.  Most people usually make the mistake of doing 
something like running fdisk and repairing errors only to get into much 
deeper trouble.


> I did run grub from this machine against the CF card on /dev/sda.  Does
> that mean I should now reinstall grub on this machine just to make sure
> the stage1 file is what I want?

That looks to be your problem then.  The stage1 file might not have 
helped as there is the possibility it contained the wrong partition 
table still.  You can run 'grub-install /dev/hda' if you want to, but I 
don't think the /boot/grub/stage1 file is actually used except 
immediately after it is created.  It's usually a good idea to make a 
backup of your MBR/partition table and store it on another disk 
somewhere.  It's very small, 512 bytes and is easy to create:
dd if=/dev/hda of=hda.mbr bs=512 count=1

If this problem ever happens again, it'd just be a simple:
dd if=hda.mbr of=/dev/hda bs=512 count=1

...to restore everything.  Some distros recommend creating a bootable 
floppy disk where they write to the start of a floppy and if things die 
again, just put the floppy in and it boots as normal.  This is not as 
common anymore with the decline of floppy drives.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

No experience, but I would start by looking at what google and yahoo
are using.  They

On Mon, 25 Sep 2006, Josh Orchard wrote:

> Hello all,
>
> I was recently asked to give an opinion about what email list would handle 
> large mailings.  They were purposing 100K to 1 Million people on the list. 
> It would be an announcement list.  I have used Mailman with great results but 
> only into the small thousands.  I've searched around but have yet to find 
> anything that compares different mailing list like Mailman/PHPList/Majordomo 
> or any other mailing list. 
> So,  is there anyone on the list who could point me towards a comparison or 
> give their opinions on which one would be a good mailing list to handle this

Are you sure these guys are not spammers?

I have used majordomo with lists of about 20,000+. I have never seen a 
legitimate list that big.

The problem is not the listserv software, the problem is having the 
bandwidth and running out of system resources.  (I hacked a custom kernel 
back when I ran perl5porters to handle high volume, but that was back in 
the days when you needed to. The system used to run out of filehandles 
back in the 1.2.x kernels.)

Josh,

Josh Orchard wrote:
> Hello all,
> 
> I was recently asked to give an opinion about what email list would 
> handle large mailings.  They were purposing 100K to 1 Million people on 
> the list.  It would be an announcement list.  I have used Mailman with 
> great results but only into the small thousands.  I've searched around 
> but have yet to find anything that compares different mailing list like 
> Mailman/PHPList/Majordomo or any other mailing list.
> So,  is there anyone on the list who could point me towards a comparison 
> or give their opinions on which one would be a good mailing list to 
> handle this capacity?

 From what I've heard, qmail's ezmlm is supposed to be very fast for 
this type of thing.  It has a lot of memory and parallelization 
optimizations and was designed for speed and simplicity.
http://cr.yp.to/ezmlm.html

There are ezmlm clones for other MTAs, but I know if they are nearly as 
good in terms of optimizations.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From sofar at foo-projects.org  Mon Sep 25 14:13:29 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 14:13:29 -0700

Message-ID: <45184679.2030709@foo-projects.org>

Josh Orchard wrote:
> Hello all,
> 
> I was recently asked to give an opinion about what email list would 
> handle large mailings.  They were purposing 100K to 1 Million people on 
> the list.  It would be an announcement list.  I have used Mailman with 
> great results but only into the small thousands.  I've searched around 
> but have yet to find anything that compares different mailing list like 
> Mailman/PHPList/Majordomo or any other mailing list.
> So,  is there anyone on the list who could point me towards a comparison 
> or give their opinions on which one would be a good mailing list to 
> handle this capacity?

for anything of this size, and assuming that your list will most likely be 
1-way directed traffic only, I would not use a ML manager at all but setup a 
webfrontend for subscribing/unsubscribing connected to something like mysql, 
and just run a script to post announcements.

I doubt that you want to archive all the returning traffic :)

Cheers,

Auke

Subject: [PLUG] scriptable web client for HTTPS POST of file ... that is NOT
	curl
Message-ID: <659b9ea30609251421h66cac2e6wc7030f63b6c5c0cc@mail.gmail.com>

I need to write a script that will hit a server, retrieve a cookie,
then send the cookie back with a request to POST a file, all over SSL.
 Does anyone know if there exists anything other than curl to do this?
 Curl works great, but this needs to run on an older RedHat 7.3
install where curl is not up to date.  I was hoping links/lynx/w3m
would be scriptable in this way, but so far I have not figured out
how.  I suppose I could write a perl script, but this seems
complicated with all the requirements.  Any thoughts?

Chris


From daniel at digitree.org  Mon Sep 25 14:27:34 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 14:27:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] scriptable web client for HTTPS POST of file ... that
	is NOT	curl
In-Reply-To: <659b9ea30609251421h66cac2e6wc7030f63b6c5c0cc@mail.gmail.com>
References: <659b9ea30609251421h66cac2e6wc7030f63b6c5c0cc@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <451849C6.0@digitree.org>

Chris Dawson wrote:
> I need to write a script that will hit a server, retrieve a cookie,
> then send the cookie back with a request to POST a file, all over SSL.
> Does anyone know if there exists anything other than curl to do this?
> Curl works great, but this needs to run on an older RedHat 7.3
> install where curl is not up to date.  I was hoping links/lynx/w3m
> would be scriptable in this way, but so far I have not figured out
> how.  I suppose I could write a perl script, but this seems
> complicated with all the requirements.  Any thoughts?

I was going to suggest that you could use wget, but I believe that 
around in the RedHat 7.3 days, wget didn't properly support SSL.  Can 
you verify this by trying to download a page over HTTPS?  If wget 
supports SSL, see if it has the following options:
   --load-cookies
   --save-cookies
   --post-data or --post-file

If your wget configuration has those options, you should be able to do 
what you want with that...otherwise I'd probably say to use perl.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

On Mon, 2006-09-25 at 14:21 -0700, Chris Dawson wrote:
> I need to write a script that will hit a server, retrieve a cookie,
> then send the cookie back with a request to POST a file, all over SSL.
>  Does anyone know if there exists anything other than curl to do this?
>  Curl works great, but this needs to run on an older RedHat 7.3
> install where curl is not up to date.  I was hoping links/lynx/w3m
> would be scriptable in this way, but so far I have not figured out
> how.  I suppose I could write a perl script, but this seems
> complicated with all the requirements.  Any thoughts?

As someone who's rewriting a bunch of scripts that used curl and
wrappers around ftp/sftp, I'll tell you that doing it in a scripting
language will be much simpler in the end--7.3 should have LWP (although
you might have to hunt to find a Crypt::SSLeay module to implement
HTTPS).

Hm, I actually seem to have (old and obviously unmaintained) RPMs of
perl-libwww-perl and perl-IO-Socket-SSL (which I think LWP can use in
addition to Crypt::SSLeay): http://ftp.nakedape.cc/nakedape/rh73/rpm/

Wil

I suspect that Google has a custom application, but I'm pretty sure
Yahoo uses IronPort boxes. They are optimized for sending email, and can
pump out 1M per hour. I interviewed there a long time ago; everything
was written in stackless Python on FreeBSD. 

Is there a time limit for getting these emails out? If you can take a
day, any mailing package will work, assuming you have sufficient
bandwidth. If you need to go faster, that may take some work.

Yeah, it's the correct URL.  I'm not really sure what is going on for 
you.  I'm pretty sure my DNS settings are correct because I've accessed 
the website from the Internet before and I've just double-checked with 
some online DNS lookup websites and they report back the correct 
information.  Try to ping projects.digitree.org and see if it can do a 
name resolution.  projects.digitree.org has been in my DNS records for 
at least a month.

For a temporary fix, try to add the following entry to your /etc/hosts 
file if the DNS resolution fails.  That should at least get you to the 
website to download the script:
63.105.26.158    projects.digitree.org

Let me know what you find out.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Mon Sep 25 20:44:49 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 20:44:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Newly assembled machine overheating
In-Reply-To: <4514C8F9.2050208@comcast.net>
References: <4514688F.60405@comcast.net>	<20060923031746.GA14083@gate.kl-ic.com>	<4514C3DB.5050107@comcast.net>
	<4514C8F9.2050208@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4518A231.6060901@comcast.net>

Renegade Penguin wrote:

> 1) Only use the white thermal grease - NOT the silver stuff EVER.

It's white.

> 2) Don't use too much.  Just a large drop is often enough.  The heat 
> sink should be doing its job, not mostly the grease.

I think I used a modest amount. (See 8, below).

> 3) If it runs fine with the case side off, your problem is definitely 
> 100% airflow.

I still get the alarm with the case side off.

> 4) If you've got room for an additional case fan (or two) - use them.  
> They're the inexpensive part of a system and as it gets older, you'll 
> need better airflow.

The case has a front fan, blowing in, and a back fan, blowing out.

> 5) Check cabling to see if you can minimize size - cables in the way 
> of airflow will SERIOIUSLY overheat a small case.  Optional - switch 
> to a mid or full tower with quad fans instead of a mini case.

The hard drive and CD cables are the round ones. The floppy cable is 
still the flat one, but it's tucked out of the way.

> 6) Heat rises.  Make sure that the fans are the following:
>    a) Front fan at BOTTOM - sucks in

Yes.

>    b) Back fan towards TOP - blows out

Yes.

>    c) POWER SUPPLY fan blows OUT.

Yes.

>    e) If there are two fans in the power supply, front or bottom fan 
> sucks in, back fan blows out

Yes.

> 7) If you've got a side-mount case fan (on the case side) - make sure 
> it's working properly as well.  Lower half mounted - sucks in - upper 
> half - blows out

No side mount fans.

> 8) Remove all the grease from proc & heat sink, start over with a 
> large drop of grease (not a small blob)

It's hard to get a single drop out of the two holes, so the drops spread 
a bit, rather than being a large drop. Perhaps I should try taping the 
old holes closed and making a single new one.

> 9) If you have an aluminium only heat sink, switch to copper.  They're 
> cheap compared to a full system.

The heat sink has copper fins.

> 10) Set the fans to run full speed

The English of the mother board manual leaves a bit to be desired, but 
from what I can determine, if I want the fan to run full speed I connect 
it to the CPUFAN2 pins. However, if I do that, I don't get to see the 
fan speed. The BIOS has something called FOC (Fan-Off Control), which by 
default is enabled. The description reads:

"If enable, this function protect user's CPU from overheating in an 
event there's a problem with the CPU FAN. The user should also plug the 
CPU FAN power on the CPU FAN1 pin If not, system will auto shutdown."

I assume that means that FOC is watching the fan RPM and will shut down 
the system if the fan stops. In my case the fan never stops, nor does it 
appear to substantially change speed.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens



From rsteff at comcast.net  Mon Sep 25 20:45:26 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Mon, 25 Sep 2006 20:45:26 -0700
Subject: Not Resolved: [PLUG] Newly assembled machine overheating
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609231800520.10493@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <4514688F.60405@comcast.net>	<20060923031746.GA14083@gate.kl-ic.com>	<4515C8B7.2090904@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609231800520.10493@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <4518A256.9010300@comcast.net>

To recap last week's episode:

Soyo KT600 Dragon Ultra Platinum mother board with AMD Athlon XP 2200+ 
processor and Thermaltake processor fan. After system has been powered 
on for a few minutes a beep alarm sounds: 1 second on, one second off, 
and continues until I shut it down. Watching the reported temperature, 
the most I have seen is 50 deg C. BTW, this is with the side off the case.

I did remove the CPU fan, saw no grease on the chip, cleaned up the 
grease from around it, added new grease to the chip, cleaned the grease 
off the fan, and re-installed it.

I went to the Soyo site looking for anything in the way of an 
explanation for the alarm. It's not 100% clear, but it appears that this 
is the ABR (Anti Burn Regulator) alarm. Recommendations include making 
sure the fan is running (I checked it visually, plus the BIOS reports in 
the neighborhood of 2700 RPM).

One recommendation is to clear the CMOS tables with a jumper. I did 
this, and then followed the instructions for setting a default set of 
entries from a BIOS command. When I told the BIOS editor to save and 
exit, I got a continuous beep followed by a shut down. When I powered 
back up, it acted normally -- at least as normally as I've seen it.

I proceeded to load FDISK from my old RH 7.0 install CD so I could clear 
the partition table on the hard drive. When I exited FDISK I pushed the 
reset button so I wouldn't continue installing RH 7. During the boot 
process I was unable to open the CD drive to remove the RH CD. I powered 
down, powered back up, removed the CD and replaced it with the SuSE 10.1 
network install CD. I got as far as the SuSE 10.1 splash screen when the 
ABR (I assume) alarm sounded. I let it go for a little while. This next 
part is a little fuzzy: I don't recall pushing the reset button, but the 
machine did reset. I watched the chip temperature display for a couple 
of seconds and then powered down. This was the first time I've seen the 
temperature above the upper 40's (it was 50).

This ABR alarm is set to go off at 85 deg C. If the chip is reporting 
45-50 deg, why is the alarm going off? If the chip temperature is really 
higher, why doesn't the BIOS say so?

Any recommendations on what to do next?

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From mike at mommabears.com  Tue Sep 26 07:35:56 2006
From: mike at mommabears.com (MJang)
Date: Tue, 26 Sep 2006 07:35:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] GRUB Error 17
In-Reply-To: <4518C2B8.4030203@comcast.net>

On Mon, 2006-09-25 at 23:03 -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> A network installation of SuSE 10.1 finished, but failed to boot from 
> the hard drive. When I did this last week the error message was number 
> 18. This evening I ran FDISK (from my old RH 7 CD) so there was an empty 
> partition table when the installation program got to it.
> 
> I wasn't sitting at the machine last week when it tried to boot after 
> the install, but I assume it went about the same as this evening. After 
> finishing all it's work, the installation program initiated a reboot. I 
> didn't take out the CD, so that's what booted. I chose the default 
> option, which is to continue booting from the hard drive. GRUB got into 
> stage 1.5, but then gave me the error 17 message.
> 
> I won't have any time to work on this until tomorrow (Tuesday) evening, 
> but I have found this in the GRUB manual:
> 
> 17: Cannot mount selected partition
>         This error is returned if the partition requested exists, but 
> the filesystem type cannot be recognized by GRUB.

I suspect most install programs write GRUB to the MBR via the
grub-install command. That command doesn't always work.

I would check your menu.lst (the commands that you can see from the GRUB
menu) from the GRUB command line. Assuming you've done the default for
SUSE and installed on one partition, this seems appropriate (just from a
quick google of error 17) http://www.smop.co.uk/node/43 Sometimes the
selected partition is wrong. (my system has been screwed up before when
the order of partition numbers don't match the order on the physical
drive, e.g. /dev/hda1, /dev/hda3, /dev/hda2)

Assuming you can boot into SUSE via its rescue mode (from the install
CD), you should be able to get into the GRUB command line from the OS,
and reinstall into the MBR from the GRUB prompt (assuming the first HD)
with the following command:

setup (hd0).

Since you've looked at the GRUB manual (ref for others
<www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/Command-line-and-menu-entry-commands.html> ) you can see what other commands you might try.

Thanks,
Mike



From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Sep 26 07:56:28 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 26 Sep 2006 07:56:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] GRUB Error 17
In-Reply-To: <4518C2B8.4030203@comcast.net>
References: <4518C2B8.4030203@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <45193F9C.5040300@digitree.org>

Richard,

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 17: Cannot mount selected partition
>        This error is returned if the partition requested exists, but the 
> filesystem type cannot be recognized by GRUB.

Have a look at the "grub and fdisk problems mean what?" thread from 
yesterday and the day before.  The problems you're describing seem 
similar, but not by the initial cause.

For some reason it sounds like your partition table keeps getting set to 
values that aren't correct, or the table is getting corrupt.  Since 
you've just re-installed a linux distro which should've written out a 
new partition table and it doesn't boot immediately afterward, I would 
suggest that it could be a problem with your hard disk geometry 
settings.  Check your BIOS settings and make sure that things are set to 
automatically detect geometry and the values look right.  I personally 
know someone who had this problem where everything went smoothly during 
the install but GRUB always failed to boot Linux.  They reinstalled the 
Linux distro about 4 times, always with the same result.  In the end, 
one look at their bios and changing the hard disk detection to auto 
fixed everything.

Also, the linux installer could be choosing bad values somehow.  How old 
is your computer you're installing on?

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

So, in sum: something's deeply and weirdly screwed-up with either
Firefox or system libraries that it uses, in a manner that is likely
unique to this one system.  It has at least two symptoms, either of
which makes the browser nearly useless to you.

Neither you, nor the tandem intelligence of many of Portland's brightest
Linux experts, have been able to resolve this issue, which has persisted
for quite a while now.

It has consumed many collective hours that could have been spent helping
others, earning one's daily bread, or being with loved ones.

Given all this, I will ask:
1. Have you tried completely removing Firefox, and reinstalling it by
whatever means recommended by the Slackware folks?
2. Have you tried removing Firefox and installing it from a tarball?
3. Have you considered trying e.g. Konqueror?
4. Have you brought your computer to a Linux Clinic so skilled gurus
might poke and prod at it instead of speculating at a distance?
5. Have you considered using a computer that works correctly, instead?
6. Again, may I send you an Ubuntu CD?

Good offences make good neighbors,
  Aaron


From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Sep 26 10:59:53 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 26 Sep 2006 10:59:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wanted: Firefox Configuration Guru Help
In-Reply-To: <20060926171411.GA16285@syrinxpc.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609260645530.10550@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20060926171411.GA16285@syrinxpc.com>
Message-ID: <45196A99.1090506@digitree.org>

Aaron Burt wrote:
> So, in sum: something's deeply and weirdly screwed-up with either
> Firefox or system libraries that it uses, in a manner that is likely
> unique to this one system.  It has at least two symptoms, either of
> which makes the browser nearly useless to you.

Yes, something is messed up.  I've figured out what it is.  I've been 
able to recreate Rich's problem and am now trying to narrow down exactly 
which little variable is causing the fuss.  I'll explain a little bit 
about what I found out so far:

The postscript document that Rich provided me does not contain any text 
except for a couple of headers.  The body of text is definitely not 
present in the postscript file.

I noticed 'certain difference' between Rich's postscript source and mine 
which is that his printer output is using different fonts...the big 
difference is that mine were using the Arial-based font from the 
msttcorefonts package.  After I removed the package (but without 
restarting Firefox), my postscript output I generated failed in exactly 
the same way that Rich's did.  The website Rich gave me 
(news.google.com) uses Arial in its stylesheet.

My belief is Firefox is not selecting a reasonable alternative font when 
trying to generate his postscript document.  Firefox (or the postscript 
renderer) either doesn't know a good alternative font and is saying 
"I'll just skip it", or there is a font definition that it is choosing 
that just doesn't work.

After I restarted Firefox, my printing returned to normal and I was able 
to print the page fine again (without the Arial font).  The postscript 
output decided to default to whatever was one of the default fonts in 
Firefox depending on whether it was going to use a sans font or a 
sans-serif.  If Firefox didn't explicitly select a default font but just 
left it as "sans" or "sans-serif", then it would drop back to whatever 
the user-wide defaults for those values were, as defined in GNOME and 
possibly also by the font server.  In Firefox, leaving the serif font at 
"serif" would result in it using the GNOME's "Application font". 
Leaving the sans-serif font at "sans-serif" would result in the 
postscript using "Nimbus Roman" based fonts, regardless of the GNOME 
fonts I chose in the preferences.  This must be set elsewhere, possibly 
the font server.

As for Rich, he probably has some bad settings in his font server (a 
font or two that don't resolve).  He can get around this, for Firefox at 
least, by setting his default fonts to something he knows that works, 
maybe something like Bitstream Vera.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Sep 26 13:05:29 2006

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Sep 26 13:48:37 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 26 Sep 2006 13:48:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wanted: Firefox Configuration Guru Help
In-Reply-To: <45196A99.1090506@digitree.org>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609260645530.10550@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<20060926171411.GA16285@syrinxpc.com>
	<45196A99.1090506@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <45199225.8080302@digitree.org>

Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> As for Rich, he probably has some bad settings in his font server (a 
> font or two that don't resolve).  He can get around this, for Firefox at 
> least, by setting his default fonts to something he knows that works, 
> maybe something like Bitstream Vera.

I've continued on playing a bit more with the font issue in Firefox.  I
have some additional contributions to make that may help Rich.

I can very easily make Firefox reproduce the same postscript generation
problems that Rich has by loading Firefox:
1. Visit news.google.com.

2. Remove the msttcorefonts package (which has the Arial font the page's
CSS defaults to) or move the '/usr/share/fonts/msttcorefonts' directory
to something else.

3. Click on "print preview".  The page contains some text (page headers
and a couple other pieces), but the main body of text doesn't exist in
the postscript.  This matches exactly with what Rich's postscript looks
like.

Restarting Firefox causes Firefox to choose a different default font
that does exist though, unlike what is happening for Rich which keeps
choosing a bad font.

I mentioned that Rich could have a bad font, so I tried creating some
scenarios that would cause bad font problems to persist across Firefox
restarts.

1. I have tried moving fonts out of the '/usr/share/fonts' path, but
when Firefox starts it just defaults the postscript output to another
font.  There are still entries in the font server's path, as checked
with 'chkfontpath -l'.

2. I have tried moving/renaming fonts but keeping them within the
'/usr/share/fonts' directory.  Firefox doesn't complain and finds the
font anyway, because the font server scans for all files and gets their
name from internally within the file(?).

3. I've tried setting fonts to have their permissions set to 0600.
Trying to start Firefox, which is set to use the font, fails to start
altogether.

4. I've tried setting fonts to have their permissions set to 0600 after
Firefox has been started but before doing a print preview.  Firefox
crashes when trying to preview.

5. I've tried corrupting fonts by inserting random data randomly through 
about 2% of the file.  I made sure to leave the header definitions 
intact that define the name of the font and its capabilities).  Firefox 
was willing to start with and use the corrupt font.  It mostly just 
displayed very odd looking characters on the screen.  The print preview 
showed the same random oddities, but not "no text".  Surprisingly it 
didn't even crash like I was expecting.

Overall, I can't seem to create a font or modify any settings that would 
cause Firefox not to detect a bad/missing font when it starts up.  I've 
had a look into fc-cache/list/cat to examine whether fonts were 
disappearing from the font lists when moving or modifying them. 
Everything appeared as I expected, ie. moving fonts out of font 
directory removed them from the list, etc.

Anyone have any other suggestions as to how the font server might 
provide a bad printer font to Firefox and/or ways to fool the browser 
into thinking there's a font that doesn't exist?

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> I've continued on playing a bit more with the font issue in Firefox.  I
> have some additional contributions to make that may help Rich.

Oops.  I forgot to include a couple of Firefox bug reports that may also 
be of interest to Rich if all of what I said doesn't produce a result:
https://bugzilla.mozilla.org/show_bug.cgi?id=266224
https://bugzilla.mozilla.org/show_bug.cgi?id=239267

Another suggestion is that there is an option in the 'about:config' page 
of Firefox called 'font.FreeType2.enable' that is set to 'false' (at 
least on my system), but 'font.FreeType2.printing' is set to 'true'. 
There is a possibility that the problem could be within FreeType and 
that Firefox is using FreeType to print the body text.  If nothing else 
I've mentioned works, it might be worth a shot to change the 
'font.FreeType2.printing' variable to 'false' and see if that helps.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Sep 26 21:08:57 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 26 Sep 2006 22:08:57 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Knoppix blank screen
In-Reply-To: <4519F81F.7070203@digitree.org>
References: <4519F76F.3080909@comcast.net> <4519F81F.7070203@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609262204450.10493@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

If the kernel command line has something like "vga=791" in it, and your
video setup can't handle it, you will get a blank screen, but the bootup
will keep chugging right along.  vesa is a good call, or at least get the
vga parameter out of the command line.

Carlos Konstanski

> Daniel Hedlund wrote:
>
> Have a look at the "grub and fdisk problems mean what?" thread from 
> yesterday and the day before.  The problems you're describing seem 
> similar, but not by the initial cause.

They sound somewhat familiar.

> For some reason it sounds like your partition table keeps getting set 
> to values that aren't correct, or the table is getting corrupt.  Since 
> you've just re-installed a linux distro which should've written out a 
> new partition table and it doesn't boot immediately afterward, I would 
> suggest that it could be a problem with your hard disk geometry 
> settings.  Check your BIOS settings and make sure that things are set 
> to automatically detect geometry and the values look right.  I 
> personally know someone who had this problem where everything went 
> smoothly during the install but GRUB always failed to boot Linux.  
> They reinstalled the Linux distro about 4 times, always with the same 
> result.  In the end, one look at their bios and changing the hard disk 
> detection to auto fixed everything.

Here's my notes on fdisk and from the BIOS:
==========================================================================
Rescue:~ # fdisk /dev/hda

The number of cylinders for this disk is set to 7297.
There is nothing wrong with that, but this is larger than 1024,
and could in certain setups cause problems with:
1) software that runs at boot time (e.g., old versions of LILO)
2) booting and partitioning software from other OSs
   (e.g., DOS FDISK, OS/2 FDISK)
  
Command (m for help): p

Disk /dev/hda: 60.0 GB, 60022480896 bytes
255 heads, 63 sectors/track, 7297 cylinders
Units = cylinders of 16065 * 512 = 8225280 bytes

From dsig at sigafoos.org  Wed Sep 27 07:55:43 2006
From: dsig at sigafoos.org (dsig at sigafoos.org)
Date: Wed, 27 Sep 2006 07:55:43 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] OpenOffice.org and PostgreSQL
Message-ID: <20060927075543.7b0a1ff9185f1b913aff056124fa7fde.2c1053db05.wbe@email.secureserver.net>

I only have the windows oOo handy so ..

When I open 'OpenOffice.org Base' I get the database wizard.  It should
come up automatically when in OpenOffice.org (with all items closed) 
you select File/New/Database.

in the 'What do you want to do' select 'Connect to an existing database'
radioBtn
then select the connection (ODBC, JDBC etc).  Select next, then you
should get the 'Set up a connection to ODBC database' page.  Browse
button will display all defined connections.

this should work the same in either environment.

DSig

SigsSolutions, Inc.

If I'm reading right, things should work now. The setup (hd0) command
should have written GRUB to your MBR. 

Unfortunately, I think I led you wrong with the Website reference. That
person configured /boot on a separate partition - but never explicitly
said so. He only implies it from the relative partition locations of /
and vmlinuz. 

If I'm understanding you right, you have - not - configured /boot on a
separate partition. (The Red Hat/Fedora default configures /boot
separately; the SUSE default does not.)
> 
> ==========================================================================
> Per the instructions at http://www.smop.co.uk/node/43 I removed /boot 
> from in front of /vmlinuz.

I'm guessing if you put /boot back, per the previous entry, GRUB should
be able to find the kernel.
> 
> Booting from there produces:
> 
> ==========================================================================
>   Booting command-list
> 
> root (hd0,1)

> splash=silent showopts

> > Daniel Hedlund wrote:

> > similar, but not by the initial cause.

> > For some reason it sounds like your partition table keeps getting set 
> > to values that aren't correct, or the table is getting corrupt.  Since 
> > you've just re-installed a linux distro which should've written out a 
> > new partition table and it doesn't boot immediately afterward, I would 
> > suggest that it could be a problem with your hard disk geometry 
> > settings.  Check your BIOS settings and make sure that things are set 
> > to automatically detect geometry and the values look right.  I 
> > personally know someone who had this problem where everything went 
> > smoothly during the install but GRUB always failed to boot Linux.  
> > They reinstalled the Linux distro about 4 times, always with the same 
> > result.  In the end, one look at their bios and changing the hard disk 
> > detection to auto fixed everything.
> 
> Here's my notes on fdisk and from the BIOS:
> ==========================================================================
> Rescue:~ # fdisk /dev/hda
> 
> The number of cylinders for this disk is set to 7297.
> There is nothing wrong with that, but this is larger than 1024,
> and could in certain setups cause problems with:
> 1) software that runs at boot time (e.g., old versions of LILO)
> 2) booting and partitioning software from other OSs
>    (e.g., DOS FDISK, OS/2 FDISK)
>   
> Command (m for help): p
> 
> Disk /dev/hda: 60.0 GB, 60022480896 bytes
> 255 heads, 63 sectors/track, 7297 cylinders
> Units = cylinders of 16065 * 512 = 8225280 bytes
> 
>    Device Boot    Start     End      Blocks   ID  System
> /dev/hda1             1     199     1598436   82  Linux swap / Solaris
> /dev/hda2   *       200    2810    20972857+  83  Linux
> /dev/hda3          2811    7297    36041827+  83  Linux
> 
> ==========================================================================

This is where I become unsure. I assume you've configured a different
directory (such as /home) on /dev/hda3. 

Thanks and keep us posted,
Mike

You should not need Windows for anything. The cable modem will connect to
your pc via cat5 to you NIC. In network setup I added 68.87.69.146 and
68.87.85.98 as DNS servers and had1.or.comcast.net as my search domain.

Renting vs buying a cable modem? I can't say which you should do. I rent
mine. This worked well for me when my original cable modem died and Comcast
brought out a new one, same day, for free.

Lance

From mike at mommabears.com  Wed Sep 27 19:02:38 2006
From: mike at mommabears.com (MJang)
Date: Wed, 27 Sep 2006 19:02:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] GRUB Error 17
In-Reply-To: <451AF1E6.5050303@comcast.net>
References: <4518C2B8.4030203@comcast.net>
	<1159281357.5048.15.camel@localhost> <451A0CCC.1000606@comcast.net>
	<1159385223.5290.42.camel@localhost>  <451AF1E6.5050303@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1159408958.5209.20.camel@localhost>

On Wed, 2006-09-27 at 14:49 -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> MJang wrote:
> 
> >If I'm reading right, things should work now. The setup (hd0) command
> >should have written GRUB to your MBR. 
> >  
> >
> I decided to give it a try. I didn't get a grub error this time. Instead 
> I got:
> 
> Disk Boot Failure

Ouch. I assume this is the same system discussed in the previous thread
as overheating. First thought is that the heat is affecting your hard
drive.

If it's still related to GRUB, one more troubleshooting hint for you -
from the boot menu, print out your GRUB stanza, and get into the GRUB
command line (Press Esc to go from the SUSE graphical GRUB menu to the
GRUB text menu, and then press c to get a GRUB command line). Type in
the commands one by one (command completion works here - it's also a
good way to test the way other GRUB commands affects your boot process).

Maybe something like - ide=nodma - on the kernel command line can get
around some hard drive issues, but that's a wild guess.

grub> root (hd0,1)
grub> kernel /boot/vmlinuz root=/dev/hda2 vga=0x31a resume=/dev/hda1
splash=silent showopts
grub> initrd /boot/initrd

If you don't get any error messages

grub> boot

should start the boot process, just as if you've booted direct from the
GRUB menu.

Good luck,
Mike



From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Sep 27 19:18:00 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 27 Sep 2006 19:18:00 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu and Kensington Exper Mouse Model #64215
In-Reply-To: <498756ed0609271810n5ffb93e4q780f2f1747e7bbb9@mail.gmail.com>
References: <498756ed0609271810n5ffb93e4q780f2f1747e7bbb9@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0609271914130.20414@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 27 Sep 2006, Victor Soich wrote:

> I have installed Ubuntu 6.06 on my laptop.  My Kensington Exper Mouse,
> which is a Trackball, connects to my laptop via a serial connection, and
> works in Windows.  When I go into Ubuntu, my Track Ball doesn't work.  Is
> there some "how to" on how to get my Track Ball to work.

   No. A trackball looks like an upside down mouse to the port. Make sure
that you have gpm running ('ps ax | grep gpm'). You can read more about it
in 'man gpm.' In X, you'll be able to set up the trackball when you
configure all devices.

Rich

Mozilla/Linux, but choose not to.

On 9/27/06, Victor Soich <vsoich at gmail.com> wrote:
> Hi all.
>
> I have installed Ubuntu 6.06 on my laptop.  As part of my "dead end
> security gaurd" job, I sometime sit in a parking lot for eight hours
> at 185th near Gresham, near Marine Drive, practically on the Columbia
> river.
>

> from my laptop while I while away my time on the job.  What kind of
> PCMCIA card should I get?  What kind of cellular service should I get?
>  T-Mobile?  Cingular?  Verizon?  Anyone have particular knowledge or
> opinion regarding service, speed of internet access, and type of
> PCMCIA card I should get?

  Cingular (ex-AT&T) has a GPRS network in town.  I suspect T-Mobil
GPRS is also available.  I've done a lot of work with Verizon's CDMA
network.  They have pretty good coverage.  The cheapest route would be
to build or buy a can-tenna and sniff around for an open WiFi AP.

> Or should I have my laptop communicat via blue tooth with a cellular
> phone, which I don't own, but might get, depending on the opinions I
> hear?
>
> Or is there some techie solution that I havn't considered?
>
> Any links I should check out?

Code Division Multiple Access
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cdma

  The Sierra Wireless support site has extensive documentation on
running their CDMA PC cards on Linux.  It looks pretty easy.  The PC
card is set up like a serial port.  Then you dial up a PPP link to the
Verizon network.

  The AirCard 555's are old and slow, but they're cheap on eBay.  I'm
not a big fan of Sierra's recent products, but the AC555 is OK.  Don't
forget to pick up an external antenna for the AC555!

Guide to the Sierra Wireless AirCard 555 for Linux
http://mycusthelp.com/sierrawireless/supportkbitem.asp?sSessionID=&Inc=698&sFilA=FAQ%20Category&sFilB=Products&sFilC=&KEY=linux

  We've been using the Kyocera KPC650 PC cards for wireless broadband
access (CDMA 1xEVDO).  The 650's seem to have the best RX signal
strength and good stability.

Kyocera KPC650 w/ Verizon Broadband
http://www.ubuntuforums.org/archive/index.php/t-77364.html

Steve D...

Victor Soich wrote:
> Hi all,
> 
> I have Ubuntu 6.06 on my laptop.  I think I want to get a comcast
> cable connection.  I have never had internet cable before.  I had
> cable TV ten plus years ago.  I think there is still some cable wire
> coming into my house.  I'm not sure.  I cannot get DSL where I live.
> I've checked various times with the phone companies, and they say that
> my part of town, S.E. Portland near Milwaukie, is not DSL capable.
> 
> Anyways, does anyone on this list have particular knowledge about what
> the best way to go about getting a comcast cable connection with
> Linux.  I'm think I've seen deals where you pay 33 a month for a year
> before they jack your prices up, but I'm not sure if I have to already
> have Cable TV, and Cable Phone.  I don't have either, and I don't want
> either.
> 
> Also, should I pay for a cable modem, or rent one, or can I get one
> for free as part of some deal?
> 
> Also, do I need Windows in order to do the initial configuration of my
> Cable conneciton?
> 
> Also, any links that would answer my question would be nice.
> 
> Thank you for your consideration.
> 
> Sincerely,
> Victor Soich
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
> I have Comcast and run mostly Linux (Gentoo).

1. If you have Comcast cable TV already, internet is an add-on. While
you're at it, you might want to look at their monthly packages, which
include moving your land telephone, TV, internet, etc.

2. There are two bandwidth options -- at least there were a year ago. I
have the high-end option and typically get 8 Mbits down and 768 Kbits up
peak.

3. If you just get internet and don't get the basic cable TV, it's
actually more expensive.

4. When I got Comcast, they provided the modem. If there's a discount
for not getting it, you might be able to save some money. I didn't
bother -- it took me a year for them to figure out how to get cable into
my house.

5. To check out the initial installation, I needed Windows. They may
have changed since then, but if you don't have a Windows box handy, you
might need to borrow a laptop from a friend just long enough to sign in
and register.

6. Once it's installed, you'll need to have DHCP properly configured on
at least one box, and if you have a home network, you'll need some kind
of router. I use one of my Gentoo boxes for that, following the
directions in

http://www.gentoo.org/doc/en/home-router-howto.xml

7. If you run into problems, their tech support people do have a few
Linux-aware geeks. Last weekend, my router decided to interchange which
NICs went with eth0 and eth1. :( The Comcast tech understood things like
"dhcpcd" error messages. :)

In all, I'm happy with Comcast internet -- you can't argue with being
able to get a CentOS 4.4 DVD at 8 Mbits/second using Bittorrent. In the
two years I've had it, there were only a couple of problems, and those
were with their DNS server.

>I really doubt that your cpu is overheating.  If I were you, I'd consider

A few days ago I went to the Soyo web site and filled out a help 
request. Yesterday, I received this response:

My comments to them:
------------------------------------------------------------------------

ProblemDescription: After the system has been powered up for a few
minutes I get a series of beeps, about 1 second on, one second off. I
thought this was the ABR alarm, but the CPU temperature is only 47 deg
C. The speed of CPUFAN1 is varying between 2766 RPM and 2812 RPM.
------------------------------------------------------------------------


Their response:
------------------------------------------------------------------------

The CPU fan speed should be between 3500-6500 RPM's 


Thank you for your understanding and patronage!
------------------------------------------------------------------------

I recall seeing a photo of the motherboard with a much smaller fan on it. I wonder if the speed my fan is turning is moving the same volume of air as the smaller fan at the higher speed. (This is one area I've actually had some experience - fans - although it was with fans that ventilate buildings, not chips! I understand that a smaller fan at a higher velocity can move the same amount of air as a larger fan at a lower velocity.) Anyway, since the reported temperature of the chip is well within the save operating range, I've followed your advice and turned off the variable speed feature thus:

The first thing I did this morning was to power up the box, enter the BIOS, turn off FOC (Fan-Off Control). Then I powered the box down, moved the fan wires to CPUFAN2, powered the box back up and booted the SuSE install CD in rescue mode. So far, so good. 

I'll now switch back to the Grub error 17 thread.

>Another thing you might consider is upgrading the

>later release.

I guess I'd better find a version number the next time I reset the box 
and compare it with what they have on their web site.

Thanks for the advice.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Thu Sep 28 10:38:45 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Thu, 28 Sep 2006 10:38:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] mangle rule to foil idiot ISPs
Message-ID: <200609281038.45145.carla@bratgrrl.com>

This seems to work on my test system, but I'd like some some confirmation from 
ye resident iptables brainiacs:

iptables -t mangle -A OUTPUT -j TTL --ttl-set 125

The idea is to foil idiot ISPs who don't want users sharing their Internet 
connections by setting all TTLs to the same value. Should be a felony, having 
your own LAN.

At any rate, does it look right?

From daniel at digitree.org  Thu Sep 28 11:28:23 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Thu, 28 Sep 2006 11:28:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] mangle rule to foil idiot ISPs
In-Reply-To: <200609281038.45145.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200609281038.45145.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <451C1447.5020405@digitree.org>

Carla Schroder wrote:
> The idea is to foil idiot ISPs who don't want users sharing their Internet 
> connections by setting all TTLs to the same value. Should be a felony, having 
> your own LAN.

Do the ISPs really care?  They have rules, but do they really care? 
There are so many problems with restricting Internet to just one 
computer.  What happens if that one physical computer is using raw 
sockets or has a virtual machine (VMware, QEMU, Virtual PC, etc) running 
and the client operating system has a different TTL?  If ISPs really 
wanted to track down how many individuals on a network, there are many 
many different ways to go about it.  Some other examples include 
checking http requests to see if multiple brower strings are present 
(ie. two versions of Internet Explorer or Firefox, epecially if on two 
different platforms).  Some http requests (at least used to) send out 
the username of the person using the computer.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

When I was with EarthLink (in NYC), they introduced their "home 
networking package," which was basically them charging you an extra 
$10/month to support your home network.  But it was really for support 
and hardware only ... you could still do your own set-up without having 
to pay extra.  The only irritation was that if you called in to report a 
problem, they insisted that your computer be connected directly to the 
modem -- if you were connected via a router (one they didn't supply), 
they wouldn't help.  Of course, their "help" always began, "Click on the 
Start Menu, ...".

gotten deleted.
